==PAGE:4==
Abstract
This thesis focuses on improving the user experience (UX) of book-tracking platforms
through the application of design thinking principles. The project begins with an explo-
ration of the existing solutions and is followed by user research, which includes an on-
line survey, in-person interviews, and analysis of user reviews from the popular platform
Goodreads.
The research identifies key elements of value for users and leads to the development of
five distinct reader archetypes based on their unique characteristics and needs in rela-
tion to book-tracking. A competitive analysis highlights the presence of alternative book-
tracking apps, such as StoryGraph, that strive to address the shortcomings of other exist-
ing platforms. Consequently, the project scope is adjusted to concentrate on two specific
stages of the reader journey: managing collections and evaluating books.
The subsequent phase of the project involves an iterative prototyping and testing process.
At the start, low-fidelity prototypes are created to gather initial feedback and refine the
proposed ideas. This is followed by multiple rounds of testing using interactive, mid-
fidelity prototypes developed in Figma. The final iteration is tested with a group of five
users, resulting in valuable insights for further development. The designers also perform
analytical testing to assess the final solution based on Jakob Nielsen’s Usability Heuristics.
The main outcomes of the project include an executable mid-fidelity prototype in Figma, a
comprehensive outline of the system’s micro-interactions, user validation notes, and com-
ments. Additionally, the defined reader archetypes, along with an adapted user journey
contribute to a deeper understanding of the target audience. Finally, the competitive anal-
ysis of the book-tracking market highlights potential areas where the proposed solution
could make a significant impact.

1
Introduction
This chapter will introduce both our personal and professional motivation for pursuing this
project, our initial understanding of the problem space, relevant terminology, our vision,
and intended goal, as well as an overview of the processes and methodology we utilized
to achieve it.
1.1
Motivation
Whether through the pages of a paperback or an e-reader, reading has the power to
transport people to places they’ve never been and introduce them to people they’ll never
meet. However, the benefits of reading stretch far beyond comfort and escape; it has
also proven to be healthy, in terms of both our physical and mental well-being. In fact,
one study found that reading is correlated with living longer, reducing mortality by 20%
among book readers compared to people who do not read books [1]. It’s also known
that reading has the power to sharpen the mind, broaden our vocabulary, and strengthen
our communication skills. A research group from Emory University found that reading
can stimulate the brain and increase connectivity in the areas associated with language
comprehension and visual processing, thereby improving brain function [2]. Reading has
even been proven to enhance empathy. According to studies from the New School for
Social Research in New York, reading literary fiction can help individuals better understand
the emotions and viewpoints of others [3].
Unfortunately, despite its benefits, leisure reading can be a difficult hobby to start for
many. Finding the right book can be a seemingly endless task, and with social media
and streaming platforms that are intentionally designed to keep you constantly absorbed,
carving out time to sit down and read a book can feel like a challenging endeavor.
This is why many who have developed a goal to read more have turned to book-tracking,
which is essentially a strategy for people to track what books they’ve read and which
they want to read. In most cases, book-tracking also involves reflecting on and reviewing
books, so as to remember one’s feelings about them later on. Apps that are dedicated
to book-tracking allow readers to do all of this - and sometimes more - in one conve-
nient place. The Insightful Reading blog produced by Bookly (a book-tracking platform)
describes the concept of book-tracking apps as a ”personal digital book inventory sys-
tem,” while also noting that some apps even ”generate statistics based on your reading
goals, the number of books you read, the time you spend on them, your overall reading
speed, and more” [4]. Some of these apps, like Amazon Kindle, are primarily digital read-
ing platforms with built-in book-tracking functionalities, making it even easier for users to
integrate both reading and tracking into their daily routines. Overall, if designed correctly,
book-tracking has the potential to be a highly effective tool for promoting and popularizing
reading for pleasure.
It was our own personal experience of wanting to track the books we’ve read that in-
troduced us to the world of book-tracking in the first place and ultimately inspired this
project. Our desires quickly led us to Goodreads, a widely used book-tracking website,
which facilitates discovering books, logging books, and documenting reviews in a large
social setting.
After trying out the Goodreads mobile app for some time, we began to notice that the user
experience was limited and, at times, frustrating. Despite this, we continued to use the
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
1
==PAGE:8==
platform due to its popularity, ease of integration with Kindle, and lack of competitors with
similar functionalities. Nevertheless, we eventually began to learn that some of our friends
had similar experiences with the app, prompting us to question whether our opinions were
even more widespread.
Our first and most immediate inclination in our efforts to find answers was to check the
app’s overall ratings in the App Store. We were slightly deterred when we found that it
had an overall rating of 4.8 out of 5 stars based on over 450,000 ratings in the US store.
However, upon brief further inspection of the written reviews, we began to notice some
trends, which led us to suspect that while many users may enjoy the app overall, they
also feel that it lacks a number of important functionalities and improvements.
Accordingly, we set out to do user research, analyze competing solutions, and study the
framework of the existing market in order to further validate (or invalidate) our percep-
tion that there exists a considerable need for improved user experience in book-tracking.
Our initial desktop research revealed that Simona Tolcheva, a writer at MUO, one of the
biggest online technology publications, recently wrote an article called, ”6 Reasons Why
You Should Stop Using Goodreads” [5]. Her main thesis was that ”as times go on and
change, Goodreads does not. This has led to many users deciding to leave the platform
- a decision supported by years of unaddressed concerns and piling issues.” She then
went on to outline reasons for leaving the platform, which pertained to its acquisition by
Amazon, a lack of important changes, old UI, and unresolved issues and requests, among
other things.
In another recent feature, OneZero writer Angela Lashbrook quotes an anonymous jour-
nalist in an article called, ”Almost Everything About Goodreads Is Broken” saying, ”It’s
generally not a good place to find new things to read... The recommendations suck... It’s
like, 100 lists telling me to read The Handmaid’s Tale and Harry Potter” [6]. She also
notes a conversation she had with Dustin Martin, a Goodreads user and software engi-
neer who claims, ”It’s just really clunky and slow... Even having the resources of Amazon
behind it, the site feels like a relic... I don’t think I’ve seen real improvement or new fea-
tures since I started using the site in 2014.” This article has received over 10,000 ”claps”
(similar to likes) and a number of concurring comments echoing Lashbrook’s assertions.
Among those with similar viewpoints is Prateek Agarwal, a writer with the UX Collective,
who suspects that the reason Goodreads hasn’t improved over the years is that it ”en-
joys a monopoly in the space of book social networks... And if there’s one thing we know
about monopolies, it’s that they don’t have strong incentives to push themselves toward
innovation” [7]. In a subsequent article, Agarwal suggests that the only way for a startup
to ”build a better Goodreads is to literally not start off by building a better Goodreads” [8].
His belief is that instead of trying to create an improvement of what Goodreads already
is, startups should target the gaps in the reader’s journey where Goodreads is weak or
absent.
Our views echo the sentiments above. Goodreads has laid an appreciable foundation for
book-tracking and has helped us - as it has many others - personally to further nurture
and share our love of reading. Having said that, we also see unmet needs, untapped
potential, and thus, a field of opportunity. Therefore, we firmly believe that there exists
a considerable need to update the user experience in book-tracking. By confronting this
issue, we hope to also advocate for more leisure reading in the bigger picture.
2
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:9==
1.2
Aim & scope
Our high-level goal is to promote the value and enjoyment of reading on a widespread
scale. We believe book-tracking is an effective tool for elevating the reading experience
and thus contributes to this goal indirectly.
With this project, we are therefore aiming to answer the question:
How might we improve the user experience of book-tracking mobile apps?
Our scope involves
• user research, including conducting online surveys, in-person interviews, and anal-
ysis of App Store reviews of Goodreads,
• analyzing data from the user research to understand major pain points and issues
of the current book-tracking solutions,
• designing solutions tackling these issues through an iterative process that includes
frequent user testing.
The intended outcome includes high-level recommendations for a new book-tracking sys-
tem, a defined user flow, and a mid-fidelity wireframe prototype of an app that has been
user-tested and includes design documentation such as an overview of micro-interactions,
an iterated user story map, and validation to confront the specified problem.

2
Background Information
The following section breaks down some of the central features and terminology of book-
tracking apps, then discusses the aspects of value they provide and the related work that
has been researched by others. Subsequently, a review of the state of the art is presented
in which we examine the strengths and weaknesses of some of the most prominent exist-
ing solutions. Here we also comment on the dynamics of their relationships in the market
of book-tracking through a competitive analysis.
2.1
Common concepts in book-tracking
To clarify some of the basic concepts and features shared by most book-tracking platforms
that we will be referring to throughout this report, we have included screenshots of some
of the main pages of the Goodreads app as an example.
(a) Discover page example
(b) My Books page example
Figure 2.1: Goodreads screenshots showing Discover and My Books pages
Figure 2.1a shows a typical Discover page, which is where book-tracking platforms present
books for users to explore. Sometimes these books are suggested based on a recom-
mendation algorithm; other times, these books belong to more general lists, such as ”New
releases this year” or ”Trending with Goodreads members.” Usually present in Discover
pages is the opportunity to quick-add each book to the user’s ”Want to read” shelf (see
the green button in Figure 2.1a). This shelf is one of three or four default shelves (also
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
5
==PAGE:12==
commonly referred to as collections) usually included in book-tracking, as described be-
low:
• The ”Want to read” shelf accounts for the books the user plans or wants to read at
some point in the future.
• The ”Currently reading” shelf includes the books that the user is reading at the cur-
rent time.
• The ”Read” shelf includes all books the user has indicated that she or he has read
or finished reading.
• The ”Did not finish” shelf includes books that the user has started reading but aban-
doned or quit reading for one reason or another. This shelf is not always included by
default for book-tracking apps, such as with Goodreads, but most smaller platforms
do include it.
In addition to the default shelves, some book-tracking platforms also allow users to create
their own custom shelves. Figure 2.1b demonstrates what an overview of these default
and custom shelves might look like, which can be called a range of terms from My Books
to My Library to My Collections. When entering a shelf or collection, books are usually
displayed in list view, with their covers visible along with some brief summary of relevant
information about the book, which differs from platform to platform. This combination of
the book cover with the short summary of information beside it is commonly referred to in
this report as a book entry.
Almost always, book-tracking applications include Book Information pages (or Book Info
pages), which essentially provide more summarized information about a book, as sug-
gested in Figure 2.2a. Although not all of it is visible in the screenshot, this information
often includes factors like:
• Title and author
• Average rating
• Number of ratings
• Number of reviews
• ISBN number
• Number of pages
• Publication date and company
• Summary of the book
• Reviews of the book published by
other users
• Similar books
• Tags or genres associated with the
book
From the Book Info page, users can also usually add or save the book to one of their
default shelves and/or multiple of their custom shelves. In Figure 2.2a, this would again
happen by tapping somewhere on the central green button.
Another major component of book-tracking is when the user finishes reading a book and
then writes a review and/or rates the book. Figure 2.2a shows an option to quickly rate
the book just below the green button. However, this can also be combined with writing
a review, as seen in Figure 2.2b. Most commonly, rating and reviewing in book-tracking
platforms is as simple and open-ended as it appears in this Goodreads example.
Please note that throughout this project when we use the word ”reading,” we refer to
reading for pleasure in printed, digital and audio formats [9].

Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking

(a) Book Info page example
(b) Rate This Book example
Figure 2.2: Goodreads screenshots showing Book Info and Rate This Book pages
2.2
Relevant literature
To explore relevant literature, we roughly followed a Prisma model using book- and reading-
related search terms (e.g., ”book-tracking”, ”reading benefits”, ”reading applications”, etc.)
and applying them in various research databases (e.g., Research Gate, Scilit, Google
Scholar, etc.) [10].
We found that although for some book-tracking might initially seem like just another data-
collecting hobby, evidence shows that it leads to improved reading habits. According
to Jafari et al. [11], reading challenges, which are a quintessential component of most
book-tracking platforms, significantly affect the number of books read by their participants.
Their study reports that users read nearly 300% more books while partaking in these
kinds of challenges and setting reading goals for themselves than when not doing so. As
previously stated, with this increase in reading comes value in the form of various health
benefits, many of which are psychological. For one, it is commonly considered a way to
relax and escape from reality [12] [13]. Even more, according to the study conducted by
Marchalik et al. [14], it can significantly reduce the likelihood of burnout among medical
workers. Bruneau and Pehrsson [15] also proved that reading can be used for therapeutic
purposes, known as bibliotherapy. Readers don’t have to spend hours with books daily
to benefit from their influence - Billington [16] states that just 30 minutes of daily reading
makes people report 20% greater life satisfaction and 23% more likely to understand
the feelings of others. Reading’s effect on empathy extends to autistic people as well
- autistic adults who read are better at forming friendships, feel less lonely, and have
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
7
==PAGE:14==
improved general social confidence [17], [18]. Apart from benefiting our health, books
help us develop our interests, gain knowledge, and improve our critical thinking ability and
creativity [13], [19]. Simply put, spending a moment every day with a book can positively
impact multiple areas of our life, implying that tools that help us develop proper reading
habits may be well worth investigating.
As it turns out, reading challenges are just one of the multiple ways that both new and
established readers can reinforce their reading behavior. Some book-tracking apps also
include achievement levels that users can unlock to receive various badges. This is most
common in platforms that double as both book-tracking and a digital reading app, such
as Amazon Kindle or Audible, likely because it’s easier for these systems to track digital
reading over print reading behavior. The basis for implementing these types of challenges
and achievement badges can be described, in part, by goal setting theory, which was first
formulated by Locke in 1968 [20]. It essentially explains that one of the major factors
of conscious behavior change is motivation and a studied and effective way to motivate
oneself is by documenting clear and sufficiently challenging goals. Other important char-
acteristics described in Locke’s goal-setting theory are that they must involve commitment
and feedback. This translates to book-tracking because users are prompted to set their
own goals of how many books they want to read in a given time frame. This allows read-
ers to have autonomy of choice, which therefore increases their commitment to the goal
they have set themselves. The achievement badges, on the other hand, are usually as-
sociated with a variety of challenges proposed by the book-tracking platform. Examples
of awards in the Audible app include:
• ”Marathoner: You’ve listened for at least 5 hours straight. Keep clearing that sched-
ule [completed].”
• ”Mount Everest: This epic award is given away when you listen to something as
long as a day [uncompleted].”
What’s especially notable about these is the feedback element. Colorful badges appear
on the user’s screen upon entering the app once each challenge is completed, instilling
in them a sense of achievement. For Deterding [21], this use of badges as feedback
would provide a solid example of gamification in action. As Deterding et al. [22] describe,
gamification is a strategy that involves implementing elements of game design into non-
game contexts for the sake of user motivation and engagement. As for the more common
self-chosen reading goal challenges, for example, the feedback is different from that of
the achievement badges in that users are often presented with a consistent progress bar
to gauge how much closer they are to achieving their goal. However, in Goodreads, for
example, the system does nothing to reward users for reaching said reading goal when
it’s complete.
Aside from providing components of motivation to help users read more, the core ele-
ment of book-tracking is implied in its name: tracking read books. Doing so through one
unified platform serves as an inventory system and can affect one’s feeling of ownership
over books, especially digital ones. As described by Watkins et al, ownership can be
considered in three dimensions: ”control – the degree of agency a consumer has over
an object in its use; continuity – the duration for which a consumer is awarded access
to and agency over an object, and finally; transferability – the ability to move an object
through its cycle of biography, either through divestment, gifting, exchange or destruction”
[23]. In the lens of reading print books, many of the books people read come and go from
their possession, possibly getting lost, passed on to others, or even returned to a library.
Book-tracking allows users to maintain a synthesized list of the titles they own, as well
8
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:15==
as those they’ve lost so that no book is forgotten. When buying e-books from Amazon to
read on their Kindle e-reader, users do not own them in the same sense as they might own
physical books. In fact, they purchase only a licensing agreement to read the books using
the Kindle app, affecting the dimension of control. They cannot sell them, give them as a
present, rent them, or transfer ownership in any other way [24]. Users also do not own the
books permanently, as, in extreme cases, when they violate the terms of use, Amazon
can suspend their access to the account and library. Although book-tracking apps are
not meant to store the content of the books, users can add information about read/owned
ones to their personal library. While e-books themselves can be tied to a specific plat-
form (e.g., only to the Kindle ecosystem), dedicated book-tracking apps usually do not
have this restriction. They allow users to have one place to store information about books
from different digital providers as well as about printed copies. This can lead to increased
feelings of ownership over their collections.
The concept of book-tracking is also an example of a personal informatics system. Ac-
cording to Li [25], personal informatics systems ”help people collect personally relevant
information for the purpose of self-reflection and gaining self-knowledge.” Most digital
book-tracking apps allow their users to track some aspect of their reading behavior, be
it the number of books read over time, their reading speed, the total number of pages
read, or other related measurements. According to various studies on the effects of self-
improvement through self-knowledge (also known as ”self-improvement hypothesis” [26]),
awareness of our habits can positively impact our behavior ([27], [28], [29]). Taking into
account the stage-based model of personal informatics proposed by Li et al [25], book-
tracking apps are usually a bit more focused on collection and integration stages than
reflection and action. This might be enough for some users, but we hope to understand
this aspect of user needs better over the course of the project.
Today, reading - despite being a primarily solitary activity - has significant social dimen-
sions ([30], [31], [32]). Book-tracking apps may have various features of typical social me-
dia platforms. The largest social book-tracking platform is Goodreads, which offers users
the ability to follow friends and view their activity on a news feed, similar to Facebook or
Twitter. According to Thelwall et al.’s research, Goodreads represents a hybrid combina-
tion of a book-based website and a social networking site [31]. As such, social presence
theory should be considered when evaluating potential aspects of book-tracking user ex-
perience. It describes the psychological sensation that other people are ”real” through
digital communication methods [33] and can be utilized to provide a more immersive user
experience [30]. An example of a medium with a high social presence is video-calling,
while emailing is a method with a much lower relative social presence. Higher levels can
result, for example, from the ability to transmit different amounts of visual cues, such as
text-only communication VS voice calls, or giving the users the ability to express their
emotions through reactions or ”likes” under posts of their feed. While working on this
project, we aim to pay close attention to the social aspects of the existing applications
and their importance to the users to understand their value.
2.3
State of the art
The most prominent existing platforms for book-tracking were found through a list pub-
lished by the Insightful Reading blog [4] and the top search results for ”book apps” in the
App Store. Current solutions in the market are mixed between digital reading apps with
built-in book-tracking, dedicated book-tracking apps, social book-tracking platforms, and
non-digital methods of book-tracking. For the purposes of this project, we will mainly be
looking at the iOS versions of the book-tracking apps, although we do recognize that they
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
9
==PAGE:16==
may elicit different user experiences on Android devices. Also, note that the ratings and
reviews reported for each app are taken from the US region’s App Store. The following
seven apps are found to be among the most popular:
2.3.1
Amazon Kindle
4.8 out of 5 stars
4,036,651 ratings
Amazon Kindle is a platform in which users can browse, purchase, download, and read
e-books. Its link to Amazon allows users to choose from over 1,000 titles and to seam-
lessly switch between digital and audiobooks. In this app, users can access their library of
purchased Kindle books as well as free samples of books they’ve acquired. As mentioned
previously, Amazon Kindle also includes a series of challenges and correlated achieve-
ments related to different aspects of the user’s reading behavior. Examples include chal-
lenges based on the number of days users read within a time frame, the number of books
completed, reading streaks, and other special objectives. The Kindle app also gives users
the option to set a reading goal and view their records of longest weekly and daily read-
ing streaks. Some of the other main features of Kindle advertised in the App Store include:
“

2.3.8
Manual Journal
Aside from the reading and book-tracking apps discussed above, we also see a manual
journal as a popular non-digital tool that many may use to accomplish similar book-tracking
activities as those mentioned above. In a manual journal, it’s completely up to the user
what is included, making it by far the most customizable solution of the gamut. A major
disadvantage associated with a manual journal, however, is that using it likely requires
substantial constant work, depending on the level of detail included.
In closing, while the ratings of the apps are meant to provide some indication of user
satisfaction, we do acknowledge that not all users rate apps, which means we cannot rely
on ratings and reviews alone to paint a full picture of the success of an app or method.
Evaluating the existing solutions is useful for us to not only gauge what already exists but
also to potentially highlight gaps where opportunities may lie.

==PAGE:25==
3
Methodology
The processes and methodologies most utilized in this project include the concepts of
user experience and design thinking.
3.0.1
User experience design
User experience (UX) essentially describes all the effects elicited from an interaction be-
tween the user and product, service, or system [34]. This includes the time before use
(i.e., when a person first learns about a product), during use, and after use. Effective
user experience design considers a product’s usability, usefulness, emotional impact,
and meaningfulness [34]. Peter Morville presents another interpretation using his User
Experience Honeycomb, as illustrated in Figure 3.1 [35]. Similarly, he claims that user
experience is a multidimensional concept comprised of the following seven characteris-
tics:
• useful - practical value,
• usable - user-friendliness,
• desirable - attractiveness and emotional appeal,
• findable - ease of navigation and information retrieval,
• accessible - inclusive design for people with disabilities,
• credible - trustworthiness and believability,
• valuable - contribution to the purpose or profitability of stakeholders.
Figure 3.1: User Experience Honeycomb by Peter Morville
The hierarchy of these characteristics should be determined by the unique circumstances
of each project. For example, in projects that lack financial resources, a simpler approach
might be to focus on usefulness and usability. An interesting way to assess usability
is outlined in Jakob Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics for User Interface Design. These
include:
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
19
==PAGE:26==
1. Visibility of system status
2. Match between system and the real world
3. User control and freedom
4. Consistency and standards
5. Error prevention
6. Recognition rather than recall
7. Flexibility and efficiency of use
8. Aesthetic and minimalist design
9. Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors
10. Help and documentation
It’s also important to note that user experience changes over time as people’s behavior,
needs, and contexts change, which makes UX design an ever-evolving space and is one
of the reasons behind this project. The structure of this project entails the evaluation of
the user experience of existing solutions, as well as the redefining and redesigning of a
book-tracking app with a thoughtful user experience.
3.0.2
Design thinking
Design thinking is generally described as a problem-solving approach, which focuses
on creativity, empathy, and iteration [36]. It also embodies human-centered design by
prioritizing users, their tasks, and the environment in which they’re in. The design thinking
process is often divided into five stages: empathize, define, ideate, prototype, and test
(see Figure 3.2).
Figure 3.2: Design thinking process
• The Empathize stage involves doing user research to gain a better understanding of
users and their needs. This can be accomplished through desk research, observa-
tions, interviews, etc. It’s important to keep in mind during this stage that often what
people think is their problem is not really their problem. Pursuing a deeper level of
empathy with users has the potential of uncovering what these latent needs really
are, which helps when confronting complex challenges.
20
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:27==
• The Define stage focuses on processing and analyzing the insights and informa-
tion gathered during the previous stage. The goal of this stage, as suggested in
its name, is to clearly define the core constraints and problems. This stage is vital
to the success of the final design solution because, without an accurately defined
core problem, the final design lacks direction and often fails. The define stage (like
others) may be revisited multiple times throughout the design process as the under-
standing of the problem naturally develops and evolves.
• The Ideate stage revolves around reframing the problem and identifying possible
solutions. It’s often suggested to begin this stage by prioritizing quantity of ideas over
quality, in order to better imagine solutions that may be outside the box. Creativity
is a crucial skill during ideation methods, which is why it can be useful to reframe
the problem in new ways and look at it from a new perspective.
• The Prototype stage is all about experimentation. Here, the challenge is to build out
quick and low-fidelity examples of the identified solution ideas with high potential.
The focus of early prototypes usually falls on the core function of the design (or
minimum viable product), which is essential to solve the core problem. Subsequent
prototypes can be aimed at testing or visualizing other supporting features of the
design.
• The Test stage is where the prototypes are evaluated. It’s important at this stage to
carefully choose evaluation methods and metrics, which are essentially how the de-
signers measure the success or effectiveness of a design. Validation methods can
include thinking-aloud walk-throughs, satisfaction ratings, counting the click errors,
etc.
While it’s written as the final step, the Test stage often re-loops into previous stages. In
fact, as previously mentioned, design thinking is highly iterative. Each of these stages
does not make up a linear path to follow so much as they provide different lenses of
working on a design project with which designers can jump between or simultaneously
work within [36].
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
21
==PAGE:28==
22
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:29==
4
User research - Empathize & Define
4.1
Introduction
As previously described in Chapter 3, user research essentially makes up the Empathize
stage of the design thinking process. Getting to know the users, their needs, and their
pains is vital to designing a successful product or service. The Interaction Design Foun-
dation describes user research as having a natural division between qualitative and quan-
titative methods [37]. Qualitative methods are said to be used for understanding the opin-
ions and needs of users, while quantitative methods are used to measure the actions
of users and test assumptions that are made. In the context of this project, we are in-
terested in learning from readers between the ages of 18 and 35, including those who
use book-tracking and those who do not. Our goal is not only to understand their opin-
ions of book-tracking and the existing solutions but also to acknowledge their behaviors
associated with it, all of which make up their user experience. Doing so will help us to
understand what is working in the current solutions, what is not, and where the oppor-
tunities may lie. Some of the general assumptions we had when entering this stage are
summarized below:
• Most readers are not (knowingly) regularly book-tracking.
• Goodreads is easily the most well-known book-tracking app for readers of print or a
combination of book formats.
• Many people find the Goodreads user interface (UI) to be outdated.
The three major methods we used for user research include surveying, interviewing, and
analyzing existing app reviews. The following sections include a more detailed description
of each method, our procedure for implementing them, the results, our analysis, and any
additional comments we may have.
4.2
Surveys
4.2.1
Introduction
We chose to create a survey because they are relatively easy to distribute and paint
(to some extent) a picture of how people generally feel or think about a topic. Surveys
can include both open-ended and closed-ended questions and responses can either be
anonymous or known. Distribution can happen digitally or in paper form, to a specific
group of people or to the general public.
More specifically, we were interested in discovering what people think about book-tracking
apps, what methods they use, what they like and dislike about them, which features they
use or don’t use, their general reading behavior, etc.
While creating the survey, we assumed that most of the respondents who report that they
do use book-tracking would be Goodreads users.
Apart from being easily distributed, some of the advantages of using surveys for user
research include:
• it’s relatively easy to analyze the data and compare responses,
• they’re quick to prepare and don’t require continuous maintenance after distribution,
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
23
==PAGE:30==
• they’re usually more convenient for participants, considering surveys can be filled
out at whatever time without needing to travel anywhere or speak to anyone.
While these strengths are important, it’s also necessary to acknowledge that surveys also
come with a number of limitations and challenges. For example, one of the most ev-
ident challenges is that survey responses are limited; active discussion cannot occur,
which prevents potentially valuable follow-up questions from being asked and answered.
Without being able to ask for clarification, researchers are sometimes forced to interpret
responses, which can lead to the insertion of their own biases, even if they’re careful.
Similarly, if the wording used in a survey is not fully clear to respondents, they will have
to form their own hypothesis of what is being asked, which may skew the variety of re-
sponses given. Some studies also point out that the type of people who are more likely
to complete a survey tend to be more interested in the topic, and if the survey is online,
respondents tend to be more tech-savvy [38]. It’s also possible that if a survey is dis-
tributed to the general public online, there may be a few completely irrelevant or off-topic
responses, which require that the data need to be cleaned before analyzing.
4.2.2
Methodology
We created our survey online using Google Forms. The survey actively collected re-
sponses from the 5th to the 9th of March, at which time we received a total of 105 re-
sponses. We distributed the survey through a number of social circles, the DTU Inside
message board, the DTU Facebook group, and Subreddits dedicated to survey sharing.
In each instance, we made clear from the beginning that our target group for the survey
was people who read in their free time.
The survey began with a few demographic-based questions about age, gender, and coun-
try. Then we asked general questions about people’s reading interests and behaviors,
such as the number of books they read per month, their favorite genres to read, and their
most used reading formats (print, digital, or audio). The survey then branched off into
three different question paths, which depended on which kind of book-tracking users the
respondents were, such as:
• Current users, who were asked about the method or app they use for book-tracking
and why, the features they use most/least often, what they like/dislike about the
method, etc.,
• Past users, or people that do not currently use book-tracking but have used it in the
past, who were asked about what they used, why they no longer use it, and what, if
anything, would potentially make them interested in using book-tracking again,
• Non-users, or people who do not and have never used book-tracking, who were
asked why they don’t and what might make them interested in trying it.
These italicized terms will be used in the remainder of this chapter when describing the
three user groups who participated in the survey.
After respondents completed the question path most relevant to them, they all returned to
a common thread of a few final questions, which discussed reading amount satisfaction,
interest in personal statistics, and influential factors for book selection. Considering the
range of potential question paths, the survey usually required between three to seven
minutes. A full copy of the survey questions can be found in Appendix A.
4.2.3
Results
Before reporting the results of the survey, it’s important to note that many of the survey
questions were open-ended, which means the responses varied from participant to partic-
24
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:31==
ipant. The findings from these types of questions will therefore not be addressed until the
following section because they require further analysis than the closed-ended questions.
Of the 104 survey respondents (1 was removed for being inappropriate and completely
off-topic), most were female (64.4%), between the ages 18 and 34 (88.5%), and lived in
either Denmark (57.7%) or the USA (21.2%) although a total of 13 different countries were
reported. All demographic information can be seen in Figure 4.1.

Of the most commonly used features, the four features that received votes from more than

Similarly but not equivalently, the most enjoyed features reported include: ”discovering
new books to read” (n=11); ”organizing books into collections” (n=10); and ”seeing what
friends are reading” (n=9). The least enjoyed features consisted of: ”reading or listen-
ing to books directly in the app” (n=11); ”participating in reading challenges” (n=10); and
”tracking different aspects of one’s reading habits” (n=7).

free time (87.5%). When presented with an array of potential statistics one could view
regarding their own personal reading behavior, the most popular choices, as seen in Fig-
ure 4.8, are: ”total number of books/pages read each year (and ever)” (n=62); ”total time
spent reading” (n=56); and ”how many different genres read (and which)” (n=45).
The respondents also indicated that the most influential factors when selecting a book
to read include: ”recommendations from friends/family” (n=76); ”genre” (n=70); ”book
summaries” (n=57); and ”author” (n=56). Lastly, almost 70% of all respondents reported
that they are interested in knowing what their friends and family are currently reading
(n=70).

4.2.4
Analysis
To analyze the survey results, we started by exporting the data from Google Forms to
an Excel sheet where we began cleaning it further, which essentially means that we did
simple standardizing adjustments wherever relevant, like with country names (e.g. ”US,”
”USA,” and ”United States” all became ”USA”), and ”not applicable” responses. We then
viewed the open-ended questions and began to code the responses into recurring themes.
For example, the question, ”Why don’t you use book-tracking?” targeted at the non-users,
received the following responses, among others:
• ”I didn’t know it was a thing”,
• ”I have never heard of it”,
• ”I did not know about it”,
• ”Never heard of it before”.
As part of our analysis, we coded these responses to all say, ”I didn’t know it existed.”
Ultimately, 24 out of 61 responses fit this theme, which can be seen in Figure 4.9. In
general, coding the responses as much as possible allowed us to create more informative
graphs so that we could better understand and make use of the data. This section will
thus report on the findings of these coded plots based on the open-ended questions from
the survey.
When the same 61 respondents were asked about what features, if any, would make them
interested in using book-tracking, the most common themes that emerged were: ”easy
to use” (n=9); ”recommendations based on past books” (n=8); ”none” (n=8); and ”I don’t

development. To analyze the content of these ideas, we decided to create a word cloud
rather than code the responses into themes.
Apart from the graphs, we also created word clouds using a popular Python library wordcloud1.
The goal of creating word clouds was to get a quick overview of the topics mentioned by
the users in their answers. An example word cloud is illustrated in Figure 4.11.

• users highly value books recommended by friends, but don’t use the apps to do so,

4.3
User interviews
4.3.1
Introduction
After distributing our survey and while awaiting responses, we proceeded to organize
semi-structured user interviews with the goal of gaining a deeper understanding of our
users beyond the online survey. We wanted the interviews to be exploratory and informal,
with a predetermined outline for guidance. Overall, one of our main assumptions going
into user interviews was that the insights gathered would closely resemble those collected
from the survey.
The main difference between interviews and surveys is the ability for us to ask follow-up
questions, clarify answers, and further explore relevant topics as they arose. Responses
are also generally more in-depth in interviews, and being face-to-face provides more con-
text in the form of facial expressions and other aspects of body language, which enhances
the quality of the insights. Another strength of user interviews is that they allow us to po-
tentially form lines of communication with the interviewees, which we can utilize later on
in the design process to further clarify, inquire, or test ideas.
Despite these strengths, we also recognize that user interviews are much more time-
consuming both to conduct and to process the data. It’s also usually more difficult to recruit
participants compared with surveys because it requires scheduling in-person meetings
(in our case) and preparing additional materials (e.g., snacks, notes, etc.). Additionally,
interviews require much higher active engagement from both the interviewers and the

Our intention was to schedule a minimum of five in-person interviews with readers be-
tween the ages of 18 and 35.
4.3.2
Methodology
Ultimately, we conducted five semi-structured interviews with different individuals in the
span of three days, shortly after posting recruiting announcements on the DTU Inside
message board, the DTU Facebook group, and a Whatsapp group message of DTU in-
ternational masters students. We advertised to all readers, including those who do not
use book-tracking, as we felt that their insights would be mutually valuable as those who
do use book-tracking. All interviews lasted around 45-60 minutes and took place in DTU
Skylab meeting rooms, with snacks and drinks provided by us. One of us served as the
interviewer while the other took notes. We also recorded the audio of each meeting to
refer to as needed later on.
The interview guide we created resembled the questions we included in the survey (see
Appendix B). As previously described, these questions referred to the users’ general read-
ing habits as well as their behaviors and opinions related to book-tracking. We also in-
cluded multiple card sorting activities within each interview to allow users to visually group
certain aspects or features, depending on their own interests or priority spectrum. As seen
in Figure 4.12, the three card stacks included common features of book-tracking, potential
statistics about personal reading behavior, and influential factors that affect book discov-
ery or selection.
4.3.3
Results and analysis
Of the five interviews, three participants were Goodreads users, one was manually book-
tracking, and the fifth was not tracking at all. The participants expressed different opinions
in nearly every aspect of reading and book-tracking. For example, the subject of tracking
one’s reading progress elicited contrasting responses, with some being enthusiastic about
personal statistics while others disagreed. One even claimed, ”It’s not about the quantity,
it’s about the quality.”. Even among the few Goodreads users, there was a disparity. One

really enjoyed the social aspect of the app, mentioning that it was their main motivation for

culty organizing books in the collections system, and the inconvenient recommendation
feature. An independent summary of each interview, with the results of the card sorting
activities, can be found in Appendix C.
To analyze the insights gathered from the interviews, we began by documenting all rele-
vant statements from the interview notes onto separate digital post-it notes on an online
whiteboard on Figma. We then mapped out the typical reader journey inspired by Prateek
Agarwal [7] and slightly adjusted it to reflect our own understanding of the journey based
on our user research and experiences thus far (see Figure 4.13).
A brief description of each stage of the process is written below:
• Discover is the stage in which users find and browse an assortment of books that
may or may not be interesting to them. Agarwal suggests there are two main types
of book discovery: incidental and intentional. Incidental discovery is described as a
passive approach where the user is not necessarily looking for book suggestions but
somehow is presented with them. Intentional discovery is thus more active because
the user is deliberately seeking book suggestions based on some aspect or other
(e.g., genre).
• Search involves the process in which readers seek additional, more detailed infor-
mation about a specific book (i.e., the book summary, reviews, number of ratings,
etc.).

Reason
count
Figure 4.10: Reasons why survey respondents book track
• Catalog is the stage when users decide they want to save a book to read later and
then add it to some unified list or digital shelf somewhere. Some may call this list
their ”TBR” or ”To-be-read” list. Others call it their ”Want to read” list.
• Decide is when readers actually choose which book to start reading. Many factors
can ultimately influence this decision (e.g., mood, purpose, availability, etc.).
• Read is a more obvious stage in which readers hopefully receive the most satis-
faction. Many parallel tasks can happen during this time to support the reading ex-
perience like annotating, taking notes, doing research on the side, discussing with
others, etc.
• Judge is the stage after (and often during) reading in which readers form an opinion
about the book. For book-tracking users, this often looks like rating the book on a
five-point scale and/or writing a review.
• Recommend is the phase where readers are able to urge others to either read the
book or not read the book, depending on their experience.
• Discuss involves readers sharing their thoughts and opinions about a book, usually
- but not always - with other people who have read the same book. This can be
through in-person conversations or online through social media.
• Reflect is the phase we intentionally added and used to replace Agarwal’s ”Explore”
phase. In the context of this user journey, this stage is about reflecting on one’s read-
ing behavior or progress. Many book-tracking platforms implement various strate-
gies of self-tracking, whether it be the number of books read, reading speed, most
common genres explored, etc. This aspect of book-tracking may be more attractive

We then proceeded to match each statement pulled from the interviews to one of the
previously outlined phases of the user journey, according to relevance. In each of these
phases, we used affinity mapping to group the statements into relevant categories, as
displayed in Appendix E. For example, Figure 4.14 depicts the Reflect stage, in which
we had a theme dedicated to reading challenges, as well as one dedicated to statements
against tracking, among various others.
We also consolidated the results from each card sorting activity, as illustrated in the light
grey text boxes at the bottom of the columns in Figure 4.15. Our findings from this analysis
will be commented on in the Discussion.
4.3.4
Discussion & Key Takeaways
When deciding which book to read, the least important factors for most of the interview
participants included book length, book sample, and book club. The most important fac-
tors for the majority were friend recommendations, online reviews, similar books, book
summaries, and online data-based recommendations. Again, we are seeing that recom-
mendations from friends and family seem to be the most popular source for book sugges-
tions. This was not only agreed upon by all five interview participants but also by 76 out
of the 104 survey respondents, making it the most influential book selection factor by far.
Meanwhile, three of the interview participants reported that they don’t use the Goodreads
recommendation feature and prefer to recommend books to others either in person or
over Messenger, which matches the survey results reporting that it was the second least
used book-tracking feature.
It’s important to note here that the three interview participants described above were
the only three participants who actively use a book-tracking app, which happened to be
Goodreads for all. This was not purposeful, as we only asked for readers. One partici-
pant was manually book-tracking using the notes on her phone, while the fifth did not use
book-tracking virtually at all. This is slightly limiting because while it does reinforce the
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
35
==PAGE:42==
Figure 4.12: Card sorting exercises were based on these three topics: features of
book-tracking, personal statistics of interest, and factors that influence book discovery
for users
idea that Goodreads is a major player in the market, as we initially assumed, it also only
provides us insights into that one app rather than other potentially popular book-tracking
apps.
Participants appeared to be split over their interest in discussing books with others. Some
seemed to enjoy talking about what they’ve read with other people, while others felt that
their reading experience is personal and does not need to be shared. For one participant,
it depends entirely on the type of book. This, along with the dichotomy we saw regarding
tracking personal statistics and reading challenges, indicates to us there may exist some
major diversity of user types that would be important to acknowledge. This was also
unexpected to us, as before interviewing, we imagined that the participants would be
unique in some aspects (e.g., reading style, book interests, etc.) but would ultimately
echo the same or similar views and opinions as each other regarding book-tracking and
the card sorting activities. Instead, their perspectives deviated greatly, which resulted in
a wide variety of insights to sort through post interviews.
For example, the reading challenge feature appeared to conjure strong emotions in virtu-
ally all users, especially from the interviews. While some were enthusiastically grateful for
its existence and how it had helped them, others were adamantly opposed, stating that
it makes reading too competitive and superficial. Regardless, we’ve noted that participa-
tion in the Goodreads reading challenge is always voluntary, meaning that it is perfectly
acceptable for users to not engage with it if they so choose.
Similarly, tracking in general is a topic of contention. In three of the five interviews, partici-
pants indicated in some way that tracking aspects of one’s reading puts too much pressure
and strain on the hobby. However, during the card sorting activity about personal statis-
tics, they ranked some of the more qualitative measurements highly while the quantitative
ones were towards the bottom. This suggests that users may be hesitant to track numer-
ical data such as their reading speed or performance, but tracking aspects of the books
they’re reading could be of higher interest. Overall, the most interesting statistics, as
gathered from the card sorting summary, included qualitative book trends, genres, read-
ing goals, and average ratings. This is one example of how our analysis of the interview
insights demonstrated to us that people often contradict themselves. We also heard par-

ticipants describe Goodreads’ reviews and ratings as being trustworthy due to their high

Regarding the reviews, it seemed that most readers we interviewed enjoyed reading them
but still wish there were improved ways for navigating them, such as through sorting and
filtering, or even searching by keywords. The same readers who read reviews indicated
that they also write reviews, even adding that they gain considerable enjoyment from
evaluating books after they’re finished with them. We believe this could be connected to
the idea of positive feedback when completing a difficult task. In this case, evaluating the
book may serve almost like a reward for users. Similarly to the findings from the survey,
one book-tracking user even mentioned to us her desire for using half stars, which would
help reflect more precise feelings about a book during the ratings.
From the five user interviews, our key conclusions are:
• even a small subset of readers can have vastly different needs and opinions,
• to account for the differences it might be valuable to establish various archetypes to
further guide the design process.

==PAGE:44==
4.4
App reviews
4.4.1
Introduction
For our user research, we decided to also analyze the reviews of Goodreads, one of
the most popular book-tracking apps available in the App Store. We chose this app as
it appeared the most frequently in our survey. We will briefly explain how apps can be
rated in the App Store, why we conducted this research, what we were looking for, and
the assumptions we had going into the analysis. We will also examine the strengths and
weaknesses of this method. Then, we will present our findings and discuss them.
The App Store allows users to rate apps on a scale of 1-5 stars and leave written reviews.
Other users can then rate these reviews as helpful or not. However, the definition of
”helpful” is not clear, and every user can decide for themselves what they find helpful.
Some users might vote on the reviews before downloading the apps, as they find them
helpful in deciding whether to download the app or not. Others might already use the app
and mean ”I agree with the review” when marking the review as helpful. Reviews shown
in the App Store can be sorted by the viewer, based on criteria such as most helpful or
most recent.
Our objective was to gather a large database of app-specific opinions that includes both a
score and written feedback, providing both quantitative and qualitative data. By analyzing
reviews left by people themselves without additional encouragement, we hoped to get a
more honest appraisal of the app. Specifically, we aimed to identify common complaints,
needs, and likes, as well as the most prevalent issues with the app.
We went into this analysis with the assumption that people enjoy the app and would,
therefore, give it high scores. However, we expected that they would still write about
unmet needs or issues they had with the app.
One of the strengths of this method is that there are a large number of reviews to analyze,
providing a wealth of data. However, we also recognize that certain types of people are
more likely to write reviews, such as those with strong positive or negative opinions, while
people with more neutral feelings may not bother to leave feedback. Like surveys, reviews
lack context and follow-up questions, which means we must interpret the data ourselves.
Additionally, some reviews were not relevant to the app, as people wrote book reviews
instead of app reviews.
4.4.2
Methodology
To conduct our analysis, we collected 500 of the most helpful and 500 of the most recent
reviews of Goodreads from the AppStore. We chose to focus on the AppStore as opposed
to Google Play, as the rating on the AppStore was much higher (4.8 vs. 3.5 in the US as
of 02-04-2023). Additionally, we are more familiar with the iOS version of the app and
wanted to be consistent in considering only iOS versions of the compared apps (we also
described only iOS apps in the state-of-the-art section).
To collect the reviews, we used a JavaScript script and the open-source library app-store
-scraper4 to simplify the process. This library performs a web request to the iTunes
RSS feed, formats the response, and returns it as a JSON object array. An example of a
response is shown on Listing 4.1.
1

By using this script, we were able to easily and efficiently gather the necessary data for
our analysis. The whole code can be found in Appendix F.
4.4.3
Results
As App Store reviews do not contain personal information about the users apart from their
usernames, we were unable to collect demographic information about the reviewers. The
data we collected only includes the score and the text of the review. Examples of the
reviews from ”most helpful” and ”recent” sorting are shown in Table 4.1. Histograms of
the scores from the raw data are shown in Figure 4.16 and Figure 4.17.
While the histogram of the scores from ”most helpful” reviews resembles the histogram
of the scores from the App Store, the histogram of the scores from ”recent” reviews is a
bit more skewed towards lower scores. Also, as the ”recent” reviews were usually less
informative and shorter, we mostly focused on the ”most helpful” reviews. Unless stated
otherwise, all the results and analyses presented in this section are based on the ”most
helpful” reviews.
4.4.4
Analysis
In this section, we will provide an overview of the methods used to analyze the reviews of
the Goodreads app.
The first step was data cleaning. The response objects returned by the API did not require
any cleaning, as they were already in a format that was easy to work with. However, the
reviews themselves required some cleaning. After a quick look at the data, we noticed
that users spell ”Goodreads” in several different ways, e.g., ”good reads”, ”good-reads”,
”goodread” etc. so we used a simple Regular Expression (regex) to find all those vari-
ations and replace them with ”Goodreads”. Apart from that, we also replaced all single
quotation marks and apostrophes (‘ and ’) with single straight quotation marks (’) to make
the data easier to work with.
The starting point for the analysis methods was a literature review by Dąbrowski et al.
(2022). We used some of the mentioned methods, but we also added some of our own.
In the end, we used five methods: sentiment analysis, keywords, key phrases, GPT-3,
and word clouds. All of these methods were implemented using Python, as it has a lot of
easy-to-use libraries for natural language processing (NLP) and data analysis.
The first method we used was sentiment analysis, which involved using the transformers
and nlptown/bert-base-multilingual-uncased-sentiment model.
This
model was trained on 630,000 reviews from six languages (English, French, German,
Spanish, Italian, and Dutch), making it a good fit for our analysis. We conducted sentiment

analysis to get a sentiment rating for each review and compare it with the user-assigned
score. An example of reviews and their sentiment rating is shown in Table 4.2.
Next, we used keywords and key phrases to better understand the most common themes
appearing in the reviews without having to read all of them. For keyword extraction,
we used the KeyBERT library5 and generated 1-, 2- and 3-word long keywords (or ”key
phrases”, but we will refer to KeyBERT output as ”keywords”). For key phrases, we used
Azure Text Analytics. Although we investigated various other methods, such as YAKE,
RAKE, and Top2Vec, they did not work as well as KeyBERT and Azure Text Analytics
or their usage was too complicated. An example of reviews and their keywords and key
phrases is shown in Table 4.3.
We also attempted to use word clouds to quickly visualize the most common themes in
the reviews. We generated word clouds using various text groups, e.g., whole reviews,
keywords, key phrases, and GPT-3 generated bullet points, usually grouped by the as-

To identify the most common complaints and needs in each review, we turned to GPT-3.
This autoregressive language model has proved very successful in various NLP tasks and
provided web API for access, so we decided that it might be worth testing. After several
tests using its chat interface, we ended up using the following introduction prompt: ”Below
is an AppStore review of a book-tracking application, Goodreads. I want to improve the
app, so I look for complaints/change suggestions from the user and summarise them
using very short and precise bullet points. Use ’-’ at the beginning of each of them. To
make them shorter, don’t use full sentences. Don’t write about features that exist and are
fine. If they don’t have any clear complaints/suggestions, just give one bullet point ’No
complaints/suggestions’ and nothing else.” together with every review. As a response, we
received a list of bullet points summarizing the most common complaints and needs. Out
of 500 reviews, GPT-3 found issues/suggestions in 300 of them. An example of a review
and its generated bullet points is shown in Table 4.4.
In summary, our analysis involved using various methods to create different views of the
data, which helped us gain a better understanding of the users’ needs and complaints.
We aimed to identify the most common complaints, needs, and likes to provide a good
starting point for designing the new app. We found that the generated bullet points from
GPT-3 were the most helpful in learning about the most common complaints and needs,
but we had to group them by hand manually. We also compared the sentiment analysis
and assigned scores and found that around 10% of the reviews had a difference of at least
2 points. Additionally, we compared data between ”most helpful” and ”most recent” re-
views and found that recent scores were much lower than the overall average app score.
Despite the different methods used, all pointed to similar topics, such as UI, search ca-
