4.4.5
Discussion & Key Takeaways
Assumptions and limitations
Before deciding on using app reviews as a source of user insights for UX research, we
had to understand their limitations and make some assumptions about their aspects we
could not precisely determine.
Our first concern related to the meaning of the ”most helpful” review. Reviews are desig-
nated as helpful by receiving votes from users. However, we cannot determine at which
point the users vote on that and what exactly they mean. We considered two possible
interpretations of ”helpful” by the voters:
• ”helpful” describing a review with which the voter agrees as the current user of the
app,
• ”helpful” describing a review that the voter found helpful in determining whether they
42
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:49==
would enjoy using the app before downloading it.
In the end, we did not find a way to make sure any of those interpretations is more correct
than the other. Both seem equally realistic, as Apple does not force voting only before
or after downloading an app. To avoid drawing false conclusions we avoided all of those
which would depend on differentiating between the two mentioned meanings.
Another issue came from the bias of the users leaving the reviews. According to the
article posted by Klein et al. in Harvard Business Review [39], consumers leaving online
reviews without incentives often express only the most extreme opinions. It means that
by analyzing the app reviews we got to know only the viewpoints of those users who are
very satisfied with the app, use it frequently (”religiously”, as one reviewer stated), and may
want to convince other people to use it or those who stumbled upon problems they found
extremely annoying and completely unacceptable, sometimes even without giving the app
a bit more time. We miss all those opinions that might express general satisfaction with
the app, yet still point out minor issues negatively affecting their experience. That trend
of extreme opinions is especially visible on the histogram of the recent reviews - the total
number of reviews with the most extreme scores (1 and 5) makes up over half of all of
them. Being aware of this bias was one of the reasons why app reviews were not our only
source of user opinions.
GPT-generated bullet points
The most important outcome of the app review analysis was the list of bullet points sum-
marizing the most common complaints and needs. Although GPT saved us a lot of time
by extracting the essence of each review without us having to read all 500 of them, we
still had to code/summarize them by hand. As we read through several bullet points lists,
we noticed that they fell into several groups:
• UI - all issues/comments related to the app’s general look and feel,
• Bookshelves - all issues/comments related to bookshelves and categorization pos-
sibilities,
• Recommendations - all issues/comments related to the recommendations system,
• Search - all issues/comments related to the search capabilities,
• Social aspects - all issues/comments related to social aspects of the app, e.g.,
friends, groups, etc.,
• Reviewing & Rating - all issues/comments related to reviewing and rating books,
• Data flexibility - all issues/comments related to the integration with other apps and/or
using the app data in other apps,
• Other - all issues/comments which didn’t fit into any of the above categories, but still
appeared frequently.
Comments on UI
Plenty of reviewers commented on the Goodreads app’s UI. Complaints about outdated
design, in general, were the most common, but some users commented on more specific
parts. There were mentions of dark and cluttered UI in several reviews. On the other
hand, another user said that the UI is too bright for them. As making the UI both darker
and brighter at the same time is not possible, we realized that the simplest solution to
satisfy both groups is giving them control over the app look with simple customization
options or, at least, respecting the operating system settings. The customization aspect
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
43
==PAGE:50==
also appeared in other reviews, as some users also wanted to personalize their profile
or increase the font size across the app. Sometimes the comments about UI concerned
only the color scheme, but sometimes they were also related to the layout itself. Although
customizing the look of the whole app might be difficult to implement and not convenient
for many users, it might be worth considering giving users the option to adjust elements
of their home screen. This could also partially address another group of complaints which
were mentioning issues with navigation. Users find moving around the app counterin-
tuitive and some elements harder to do than they should be. The navigation should be
improved by itself, but customization of the home screen would allow users to add short-
cuts for the activities most important for them and thus make the navigation easier even
if the layout created by the developers is not optimal for their specific use case.
Comments on bookshelves
Comments on the bookshelves were much more varied than the interface ones, however,
definitely the most popular issue from both ”recent” and ”most helpful” reviews was the
lack of a simple ”Did not finish” (DNF) shelf. Although Goodreads support explains in their
knowledge base how to do it6, it is not intuitive and many users think it is not possible to
have any sort of DNF shelf in the app.
The other group of bookshelves-related issues revolved around their privacy settings. By
default, Goodreads creates three bookshelves (i.e., ”Want to read”, ”Reading”, ”Read”),
but users can add more, custom ones. Unfortunately, none of them (including the default
ones) have any way of setting the visibility - all the bookshelves of the users are public
and visible to every user of the app. On the other hand, the bookshelves can be only
edited by their owner. This is problematic for another group of users who want to have
shared bookshelves and be able to edit them together with their friends.
There were also other issues related to the bookshelves, but they were not as common
as the ones mentioned above. Some users wanted to be able to add books to multiple
shelves at once, while others wanted to be able to add notes to the books, especially the
ones on the ”Want to read” shelf. Another group of users wanted to have an easier way of
navigating through the shelves, e.g., by being able to reorder books, filter them by shelves
or tag them. There were also some usability issues, but they seemed to be bugs rather
than design issues.
In general, the whole idea of ”bookshelves” seems to be used frequently by the users,
hence so many reviews are mentioning them. However, the majority of the issues are re-
lated to relatively small improvements in the existing implementation, rather than a com-
plete overhaul of the concept. This means that the app could be improved by simply
adding the missing features, rather than completely redesigning the bookshelves.
Comments on recommendations
Many of the reviews mentioned recommendations. The issues there were a lot more
focused than those about e.g., bookshelves. Different users were often mentioning very
similar problems.
The most common complaint was that the recommendations were not personalized enough.
Although Goodreads support explains in their knowledge base which information affects
6https://help.goodreads.com/s/article/How-do-I-create-a-shelf-for-a-book-I-couldn-t-finish-
1553870934201 (accessed: 2023-03-28)
44
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:51==
the recommendations7, it is not clear to the users what exactly is used to generate the
recommendations while using the app. Some of them assume that e.g., following specific
authors should positively affect the recommendation system while, according to the men-
tioned website, it does not. This is a very important issue, as the users who are not aware
of the fact that the recommendations are not personalized might be disappointed by the
recommendations they get. This is especially important for users who are not sure what
they want to read next, but also don’t want to waste their time on books they will not like.
Another group of users wanted to have more control over the recommendations by ex-
cluding certain books, authors, or genres. While marking the book as ”Not interested” is
possible via the website, it is not possible to do it via the app.
There is also a paradox with the DNF solution proposed by Goodreads and the recommen-
dation algorithm - if the user adds a book to the DNF shelf, it will become more likely that
they will get recommendations for similar books, as, according to the mentioned knowl-
edge base, the app ”recommends based on each shelf you create”.
The last group of issues related to the recommendations was related to the search/filter
options. Currently, the app displays recommendations grouped by genres, but that is the
only way to filter them. Users often want custom filtering/sorting options, e.g., by author,
rating, or genre which is not possible to do currently.
Definitely, recommendations are one of the most important features of the app, so it is
important to make sure that they are as good as possible. This is why the app should
clearly explain to the users what information is used to generate the recommendations
and how they can improve them. Also, simply adding more search/filter/sorting options
would be a good improvement.
Comments on search
As, in essence, Goodreads is a huge database of books, it is important to have good
search functionality. Unfortunately, the current solution is not satisfactory for many users.
The most common complaint is that there are too few options to search by. Currently, the
app only allows searching by title, author, or ISBN. This is not enough, as the users often
want to search by other parameters, e.g., language, keywords, editions, or tags. Also,
the results are not sortable in any way.
Goodreads also has a feature called ”lists”. These are public lists of books created by the
users which can be used as a source of reading recommendations. They can be of mixed
length, some reaching over a thousand books or more. Therefore, users often also want
to be able to search inside those lists, without having to scroll through hundreds of books
manually.
On top of that, many complaints, especially among the ”recent” reviews, mentioned vari-
ous bugs and performance issues with the search functionality. Although it is not a design
issue, it should be noted that features that are used the most frequently should be the most
stable and performant.
Comments on social aspects
For social comments we considered all the reviews mentioning friends’ activity, newsfeed,
profile settings, and privacy options.
7https://help.goodreads.com/s/article/How-Can-I-Improve-my-Recommendations (accessed:
2023-03-
28)
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
45
==PAGE:52==
In general, many reviewers expressed a lack of control over their privacy settings and the
things they publish on the app. There are no settings e.g., to make reviews private and
limit which activities get published on the newsfeed. To make it worse, there is also no
way of deleting the activity posts after they get published. This lack of control over the
newsfeed works both ways - users can’t control what gets published there, but also can’t
control what they see there. Multiple users complained about seeing too many ads or
irrelevant posts instead of things they like, e.g., the activity of their friends.
Another group of reviewers was unhappy with the limited options to customize their pro-
files. Currently, user can change their personal information, like name, profile picture, or
bio. However, users want to decide on what book-related information they display on their
profiles - some of them want to show favorite books or authors, while others want to hide
the specific titles.
The last group of issues connected with the social features considered Goodreads’ ca-
pabilities as a social media platform. Many people complained about simple things, like
the inability to cancel friend requests, no option of tagging other users in posts, difficulties
with unfollowing people, or too many bots/spam accounts. There were also comments
on limited discussion/reply options under the reviews, e.g., the lack of a feature to reply
directly under the comment to create a thread. We also found mentions of a user missing
friend recommendations based on their common reading history or habits. An interesting
idea that appeared also in the surveys was the concept of visualizations summarizing e.g.,
reading over the last year, similar to a marketing campaign by Spotify (Spotify Wrapped),
which can be then shared on various social media platforms.
Comments on reviews & ratings
Another big group of issues was related to the reviews and ratings. Definitely, the most
frequently appearing feature request among the reviews and on various Goodreads fo-
rums is an extension of the rating system to include half-stars. A simple 1-5 scale is not
enough for many users, as they want to be able to express their opinions more precisely.
Another concept that appeared concerned rating specific aspects of the book, like e.g.,
the plot, characters, writing style, etc. It would allow the users to be more granular in their
ratings and also make it easier to find books that are similar to the ones they like.
Users often also get frustrated by the fact that reviews are only saved after being pub-
lished. There is no concept of a working copy/draft which can be saved and edited later.
This is especially problematic for users who want to write long reviews, as they have to
write everything in one go, which is not always possible. It also makes it harder to write
reviews on the go, as the users have to remember everything they want to write. Addi-
tionally, it forces the users to write their reviews only after they finish the book, possibly
forgetting some aspects which were thought-provoking in the moment of reading.
Many users mentioned issues similar to those of the social aspect or search function -
they had problems with searching/sorting/filtering reviews, e.g., by rating, length, or date.
Also, sometimes they do not always want to display their opinions publicly, keeping them
private or visible only to their friends.
Plenty of comments about reviews also mentioned suggestions for improving the review
writing experience. Although users write reviews, for some of them a kind of guidance
would be helpful to write them better. For example, some users would like to see a list
of questions that they should answer when writing a review. This would help them to
structure their thoughts and write more meaningful reviews. It would also solve another
46
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:53==
problem - reviews being too long and not getting to the point or too short and not providing
useful information.
Last aspect of the reviews which was mentioned pertained to review moderation. Users
complained about the fact that the reviews are not moderated, which leads to spam, low-
quality reviews with offensive content. As it is a general issue with the internet, it is not
surprising that it also appears on Goodreads. However, it is still a problem that should
be addressed. Currently, users can report reviews, but the number of moderators is lim-
ited, so they can’t process everything quickly enough. One possible solution would be to
use machine learning to detect potentially offensive content and automatically hide it in a
similar way it is done on e.g., Twitter.
Comments on data flexibility
The last group of issues considered data flexibility in Goodreads - e.g. how easy it is
to integrate it with other services or how easy it is to modify the data without using the
Goodreads app.
Many users mentioned the need for integration with various applications. While Goodreads
allows its users to export the library and personal data to CSV files, it does not expose
any form of API for other application developers. Therefore, other developers are not able
to extend Goodreads functionalities by creating e.g., widgets or plugins. It also makes it
impossible to use data stored by Goodreads in other applications. That isolation of the
data is problematic for some users who want to keep using Goodreads due to its large
community, but also want to use other applications to manage their libraries, for example.
The other issue mentioned several times was the lack of an offline mode. Currently, the
Goodreads iOS app only displays an error message when the user tries to access the app
without an internet connection. It makes its usage annoying in areas with poor internet
connection and completely useless in situations when a user does not have an internet
connection at all, like on a plane or in remote areas, e.g. during trips. While some features
of the app undoubtedly require an internet connection, like searching for books, others,
like displaying the library or updating reading progress, could be easily cached and made
available offline.
The last issue in this group pertained to adding custom books/articles/literary work in
general. Currently, Goodreads allows users to add only books that are already in the
database. If a user wants to add a book, they have to create a request with all the book
data and have it approved by the moderators (”librarians”). Even though it helps in some
scenarios, it does not work in situations when the user wants to add something which is
not a book, e.g., a scientific article, or if they do not want to make the added book public.
Users should be able to add any kind of content to their library, without having to wait for
the approval of the moderators.
Other comments
Other comments which appeared very frequently were not related to specific features,
but rather to the general Goodreads experience. Many users complained about the app
being slow and laggy. They were mentioning that particularly often in the context of the
search function, but also as a problem with the navigation across the whole app. Also,
there were plenty of mentions of a whole variety of bugs across the whole system.
Another commonly mentioned need was related to active reading - users often want to
have a way to save/store their favorite quotes, highlights, or thoughts about the book.
Goodreads partially supports it by allowing to add comments while updating the reading
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
47
==PAGE:54==
progress, but they are public, which is not always desirable. Also, it is not possible to add
comments to specific pages, which is a problem for users who want to highlight specific
parts of the book.
Analysis of the bullet points generated by the GPT model was definitely the most profitable
for us for discovering concrete issues of the users and the pain points they experience
while using the app. Apart from the multiple concrete problems mentioned above, it can
be easily noticed that they span various functionalities of the app. While some of the
reviews ask for new features, which is completely normal for any application, many of
them comment on very poorly working basic functionalities, like searching or cataloging
the books. Even though they get pointed out quite often, usually with concrete solutions
from the users, for some reason developers choose not to fix them. It is quite surprising,
as Goodreads’ parent company, Amazon, definitely has resources at its disposal, so a
shortage of e.g., developers should not be a problem.
Although this method was very helpful, it has several limitations. First of all, it was not
deterministic and sometimes returned incorrect/misleading results. One of the issues
easiest to spot concerned ignoring parts of the introduction prompt which gave instructions
on handling completely positive reviews. According to it, the model should return one
bullet point with very concrete wording stating there are no issues mentioned in the review.
However, it was failing to do so in two common ways:
• sometimes the wording was incorrect, which was causing issues with automated
parsing of the response,
• sometimes it returned bullet points for a completely positive review, mentioning all
the good things the user wrote about the app, then adding a ”No issues found” bullet
point at the end; quite often also using incorrect wording.
Those mistakes were usually easy to spot in the last stage of our analysis, the manual
grouping. Unfortunately, another problem was harder to notice without reading the whole
review. Several of the ”most helpful” reviews are about books instead of the app. We did
not expect that while creating the introduction prompt, therefore GPT model was behaving
in a completely unpredictable way when given those - it was making up a whole list of
perfectly reasonable issues which were, obviously, not related to the review at all. An
example is shown in Table 4.5. Although we were able to spot those made-up bullet
points several times during the manual grouping, it is possible that some of them went
unnoticed, adding artificial issues to our list. Another issue with using OpenAI web API
was purely technical. It happened several times that the OpenAI server did not respond
to our request which broke the script execution. As, in the beginning, we were making
the requests as quickly as possible, it was likely due to a DDoS protection mechanism.
Fortunately, after artificially reducing the number of requests per second, we managed to
circumvent the problem.
Other analysis methods
Other analysis methods (keywords, key phrases, sentiment, word clouds) were not as
helpful in getting concrete improvement ideas as the solution employing AI, but they
helped us to understand our data better and direct further investigation.
Sentiment analysis showed us an interesting thing about many of the reviews - many
users assigned the highest score to their review (5 stars), yet they still pointed out some
problems with the app, thus getting a lower sentiment rating. Usually, the differences
were small (+/- 1) or none at all, but in around 10% of ”most helpful” reviews users gave
a score at least 2 points higher than the one calculated from the sentiment of the text
48
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:55==

they wrote. This assured us that even if people like the app, it has issues that need to be
investigated and all of the reviews have to be taken into account; not only those with lower
scores. Also, differences sometimes came from the possibility of editing the reviews - in
some reviews, users added an ”Edit” line at the end of a negative review stating that their
issues have been addressed by the developers and they are changing their rating from
e.g., 3 to 5. The simple model we used was not able to detect this complete change of
attitude due to the last sentence which led to scoring differences in several places.
When it comes to keywords, key phrases, and word clouds, we were hoping for getting a
quick understanding of the most common themes in each review. After generating key-
words and key phrases, we visualized them using word clouds. Unfortunately, usually,
displayed words were not very informative, e.g., Figure 4.19 shows a word cloud gener-
ated from key phrases for a sentiment rating equal to 5. It is hard to get any useful insights
apart from seeing that many reviews in this group mention ”review” and ”friend”. As they
are missing negations, it is impossible to quickly understand if reviews mention them in a
positive or negative context. However, the results of the word clouds and seeing the most
frequently occurring words helped us a bit in the later stages of grouping bullet points
created by GPT.
Key Takeaways
In summary, the key findings from the app reviews show that users:
• commonly complain about the outdated UI,
• desire a ”Did not finish” shelf,
• want more control over the privacy of shelves or reviews,
• are not satisfied with the app-generated book recommendations,
• would like to have more flexibility in searching, sorting, and filtering the books and
reviews,
• would like more control over profile customization,
• find the general five-step rating scale insufficient for expressing their opinions,
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
49
==PAGE:56==
• find writing book reviews intimidating,
• express a need for better integration with other applications,
• are dissatisfied with the app performance.
4.5
Archetypes
The insights we gathered from our user research led us to recognize a number of different
types of readers with different behaviors, needs, and goals. We decided to use these
insights to create archetypes, which would help us consistently refer to these common
user characteristics throughout the design process. While similar to personas, archetypes
are essentially clusters of user types based on key behaviors and differences without the
addition of personal characteristics that humanize the trends [40].
To form them, we began to map out some of the major distinct trends as they emerged in
our analysis of the survey, interviews, and app reviews, which can be seen in Figure 4.20.
From here, we put together different combinations of these characteristics to generate
archetypes of readers based on what we had most commonly seen in our research.
Below, we elaborate further on the six components that we first identified as making up
the major types of readers:
• Privacy - We found that readers are generally either more social with their reading
or they consider it to be more intimate or private. Social readers are more likely
to discuss the books they’ve read, be interested in seeing what they’re friends are
reading, and rate or write reviews of books. Private readers, of which there are
plenty, view reading as more of an intimate experience with themselves. This is
important to call out because with some apps, like Goodreads, the social aspect
is so central that it essentially alienates a large part of the reading community who
prefers to hold some level of control over the privacy of their reading activity.
• Format - The two most common format types used by the readers we surveyed are
print-only and a combination of print and e-books. In the context of this project, we
feel that it’s important to recognize that most readers enjoy reading print books, at
least partially, if not exclusively. This affects the importance of implementing an e-
reading feature directly in the app since it does not appear to be an obvious necessity
for most.
• Frequency - We find that most readers fall into one of the following three cate-
gories in regard to their reading frequency: occasional readers; frequent readers;
and binge readers. While frequent readers are the most consistent, binge read-
ers tend to go through periods of reading often and periods of reading very little.
This information influences the potential use expectations of the app. For example,
whether it is being opened multiple times a week or just a few times per month, this
should be taken into account when we consider what features and functionalities to
incorporate in the design.
• Intention - Our user research revealed various reasons why people read in their free
time. We have chosen to divide these reasons into two major categories. Casual
readers choose to read mainly for the mere purpose of entertainment and relaxation,
without a specific goal or intention. Goal-oriented readers have another layer of pur-
pose behind their reading, whether it’s to learn something new, to meet a previously
set goal, to develop oneself, etc.
50
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:57==
• Taste - Readers’ preferences range greatly, but we feel that it would be valuable
for us to differentiate between three broad taste categories: mainstream readers;
fixed-taste readers; and exploratory readers. Mainstream readers can technically be
associated with either fixed or exploratory taste, depending on the reader, however,
we chose to separate this category because it is common within book-tracking apps
for mainstream or trending books to be highly featured or frequently recommended
to users. Our research also suggests that users tend to have a more challenging
time discovering or receiving book recommendations that are more niche and not
already bestsellers.
• Engagement - The final element we identified was the level of engagement readers
have with the text. Active readers are more likely to annotate, take notes, underline,
do external related research, and engage with the book outside of just reading.
Passive readers, on the other hand, tend to stick simply to reading and internally
digesting the content of a book without taking further action. This factor translates
more or less into the level of expected engagement with the app specifically during
the reading phase of the user journey.
Then from various combinations of these factors that reflected what we most commonly
saw in our user research, we developed the following five archetypes outlined in Table 4.6.

Table 4.7 lays out a more detailed summary of each archetype. We recognize that not
every reader will perfectly fit into one classification or another, but by generating five typ-
ical user representatives, we can try to better empathize with the different types more
frequently. Otherwise, we could potentially find ourselves designing through one distinct
lens of our own biased view of what a user would think or do.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
51
==PAGE:58==
Table 4.7: Summaries of the five reader archetypes
Reader Archetype
Summary
Power Readers
Power readers are those who are proactive and serious about their
reading practice. They do not usually feel confined to one set of
genres and they are eager to learn from the books they read or
gain new perspectives.
Print-only Readers
Print-only readers are considered slightly more traditional. They ap-
preciate the physicality of print books and the sensory experience
that goes along with them. They are not often concerned with shar-
ing their reading experience with other people and while they read
frequently, they prefer to not add further pressure to their reading
by attaching further goals or expectations to it.
BookTok Readers
Booktok readers are generally tuned in to social media and are thus
social readers by nature. These readers are often relying on rec-
ommendations online and bestseller lists to choose their next book.
They can be driven by various goals such as being able to discuss
a popular book with friends or hitting a certain reading goal by the
end of the year.
Self-help Readers
Self-help readers are those who read occasionally with the goal of
bettering themselves in some way, whether professional, personal,
or other. These people tend to see reading as a more intimate
experience and gravitate towards psychology and philosophy self-
development books.
Novice Readers
Novice readers are essentially new to the hobby but are making an
effort to improve their habits over time. While some novice readers
may like the social aspect of reading, many consider reading to be
a more personal activity.
4.6
Revisiting Existing Solutions
During our user research, we were able to uncover more of the features and characteris-
tics of book-tracking systems that users find important or valuable. We then used these
insights to refer back to our study of the existing solutions in the market and further expand
the competitive analysis from Table 2.1 to reflect these findings. The updated results are
presented in Table 4.8. We also used this table as a tool to allow us to ascertain where the
opportunities lie for improving the user experience of book-tracking. We have included
our solution highlighted in yellow at the right end of the table to signify what we aim to
achieve in designing our solution. A comprehensive competitive analysis, which includes
both the original table and the added section, can be found in Appendix S.

==PAGE:65==
While revisiting the existing solutions with our new understanding of user needs and val-
ues from the user research, we were particularly surprised by StoryGraph. The more we
explored its features and functionalities, the more we believed it was made in response
to Goodreads and its wide list of limitations. For example, StoryGraph supports half-star
ratings, ”Did not finish” collections, and a better shelf search, sort, and filtering system.
These are all requested features that appeared various times within the Goodreads app
reviews and during our interviews with Goodreads users.
Although StoryGraph is equipped with a number of these desired features, we noticed
through exploring the app and its reviews that a few of these elements are underdevel-
oped. For example, although reviews are more structured, they are difficult to browse,
sort, or filter. Even though StoryGraph is young (2019) and clearly strives to improve
the correct areas, we believe it has attempted to implement too many features at once.
Regardless of its potential, we surmise that without a proper strategy for successfully de-
veloping a minimum viable product before expanding its functionalities, it will not be able
to compete with Goodreads in the future.
This analysis of how StoryGraph compares to Goodreads in terms of both included fea-
tures and development stage triggered a critical pivot within our design process. Where
we had initially envisioned ourselves developing the foundation for a new app with an
improved user experience, at this point we realized that we needed to narrow our scope.
In order to produce a solution of quality, we would not be able to address all aspects of
book-tracking at once. If we wanted to stand apart from solutions like StoryGraph, we
realized we needed to foster the growth of one or two major elements of book-tracking
before looking onward.
The two areas we chose to focus on include managing collections and evaluating books.
Managing collections is arguably the most central activity of book-tracking; it involves
tasks such as adding books to collections or shelves, browsing said shelves, and other
general interactions with the collections. We also found that managing collections was a
frequent topic of discussion in our user research. Many indicated that the collections in
their current system need to be updated and lack a number of seemingly obvious features
(e.g., Goodreads doesn’t provide a default ”Did not finish” shelf and StoryGraph doesn’t
support custom book collections). Therefore we felt that managing collections would be
an obvious aspect of book-tracking to include in our narrowed scope for this project.
Additionally, we were drawn to include evaluating books, which includes both rating and
reviewing after reading them. We found that most users of book-tracking interact with
ratings and/or reviews consistently, whether by writing their own or reading others’ sub-
missions. We also noted that Goodreads and StoryGraph operate at two different ends
of the spectrum in terms of providing assistance with this activity. Where Goodreads
presents users with a simple five-step star rating scale and an open-ended text box with
the placeholder text ”What did you think of the book?”, StoryGraph launches users into a
complex form of 11 distinct questions. These questions are designed to gather more spe-
cific and descriptive thoughts and opinions about the books, but consequently may also
serve as a barrier to some users who would otherwise write a review but are instead too
overwhelmed and unsure how to interpret certain phrasing (e.g., ”Is this book mainly plot-
or character-driven?). This polarity between the user experiences of these two platforms
intrigued us, which is why we selected it as our second focus area for this project.
The competitive analysis in Table 4.8 thus reflects our narrowed scope through the ”X”
marks in the highlighted column, which refer to elements in either of our two focus areas:
managing collections and evaluating books.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
59
==PAGE:66==
4.7
Problem Definition
From our user research, we learned that two of the core functionalities of book-tracking
include managing collections and evaluating books. Managing collections is often overly
complex, limited, and unclear, while the process of evaluating books is either considered
rigid and inflexible or difficult to navigate and confusing. Therefore, in the context of this
project, we have asked ourselves the following question:
How might we make managing collections and evaluating books more intuitive,
flexible, findable, and easy to navigate for both new and existing book-tracking
users between the ages of 18 and 35?
To clarify:
• intuitive - usable without needing instruction or direction,
• flexible - adapting to unique user preferences and providing freedom to sufficiently
express user views,
• findable - enabling easy way of locating essential features/functions,
• easy to navigate - easy to move around.
These terms conform to various facets of the User Experience Honeycomb [35]. Intu-
itive corresponds to usable, which can be further guided by Jakob Nielsen’s ”10 Usability
Heuristics” [41], and flexible corresponds to useful. Both findable & easy to navigate cor-
respond to findable from the honeycomb but we have distinguished them to emphasize
the difference between the visibility of important features and navigation of the app, re-
spectively.
In the following chapters, we attempt to answer this question while closely considering
the lenses of the five reader archetypes we developed from our user research.
60
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:67==
5
Iterations - Ideate, Prototype & Test
5.1
Introduction
After arriving at a place in our user research in which we felt we had developed a solid
foundation of understanding of our users and a specified problem direction, we began
prototyping and testing by following a build-measure-learn cycle strategy. In this chapter,
we therefore present four of our iterations using the following structure: what (as in what
did we test), how (as in how did we test it), and outcome (as in what was the outcome). The
fifth and final iteration is presented and discussed more broadly in the following chapter.
5.2
Process
While doing user research, we occasionally generated various solution ideas. These
premature, brief ideas were consistently documented in an ”idea parking lot” (see Ap-
pendix G). As we transitioned into the design phase with a more specified problem space,
we did an additional brainstorming session (first independently, then together) to gather
as many ideas as possible for the first round of testing. Results of this brainstorming can
be seen in Figure 5.1. Based on our project’s two domains, we were able to thematically
categorize our assortment of ideas into 11 focus areas presented in Table 5.1.

We used these focus areas to guide our first iteration. After the first iteration, we began
developing and adjusting a User Story Map (USM), which helped us map out the activities
and tasks the users would need to accomplish to meet their goals when using a book-
tracking system.

In each round of testing, participants were first given an initial survey in which we asked for
their demographics (gender and age), whether or not they actively use book-tracking, and
a series of questions to better understand what type of reader each of them were based
on the archetypes we identified during our user research. To view the full survey template
and all resulting filled-out surveys, see Appendix H. The final archetype classifications for
each user we tested with are listed in each of the following iteration sections.
5.3
Iteration 1
5.3.1
What was tested
Our first iteration focused primarily on exploring different undeveloped ideas and their
initial appeal to the users to decide which to further evolve. For example, we explored:
• different shelf and library views (see Figure 5.2),
• possible shelf sorting approaches,
• ”up next” reading queues, in which users could line up their next reads,
• in-shelf book recommendations,
• private quick-notes appended to books to provide context while adding them to a
shelf,
• different methods of quickly adding a book to a shelf from the Discover page,
• home screen widget, which would allow the user to quickly log a new title or progress,
• shared shelves, which would allow users to collaborate and co-create shelves with
their friends, an alternative method for quickly and easily recommending books to
friends (see Figure 5.3),
• different scale options for book ratings,
• rating multiple book aspects (as opposed to a single overall rating)
• predicted book rating informed by user interests,
• question prompts to guide the book review process (review prompts),
• different privacy options of published reviews.
To see more sketches and initial prototypes check Appendix I.
5.3.2
How it was tested
To test these early ideas, we organized a small and informal focus group in which we
facilitated a discussion between three participants. The goal was to receive feedback on
62
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:69==
(a) Books displayed in a list
(b) Books displayed in a grid
(c) Books displayed on a shelf
Figure 5.2: Sketches of different options to display books inside a collection
the existing concepts and co-create new ideas together. A one-page testing plan that we
created can be found in Appendix J. The prototypes we created for testing were rough
sketches using two digital drawing tools (compare Figure 5.2 and Figure 5.3). We inten-
tionally chose to do this first round of testing with a group of a few people rather than
testing with one person at a time because the prototypes were not very detailed at this
point and we felt that the potential discussion among a group that is comfortable speaking
up around each other would be more valuable in this case than their individual feedback.
This also allowed them to build off of each other’s ideas when co-creating rather than
feeling like they’ve been put on the spot.
5.3.3
Outcome of the tests
The results from the surveys indicated that two of the three users were an equal blend
of the Novice and Print-Only reader archetypes. The third participant was most closely
matched with the Print-Only archetype. From our discussions, we received a number of
comments, ideas, and conclusions, which we then used in creating our second iteration.
The main insights are described below.
With respect to the library and shelf views, we learned that participants would prefer to
have the option to toggle between a list and a grid view within a shelf. This would therefore
avoid the need to design only one layout, which limits the type of value offered to the user
(e.g. the list view can include short summaries of information while the grid view can
provide a more visual overview of the books in the shelf). Similarly, participants also
indicated that the Library page (or overview of shelves) should display book covers rather
than using some other imagery such as folders or book spines with only titles listed as
they would appear on a physical bookshelf.
The shared shelf concept as a simplified friend recommendation system was well-received,
but it was mentioned that it would be useful if users could include a short note with each
book entry about why it was added to the shelf. This would allow other collaborators to
understand the context or reason for the recommendation better.
To quickly add books from the Discover page into a shelf, users shared that they would
instinctively look for a button or icon rather than think to long-press or swipe on a book

entry. This was especially interesting because one of their specific comments was that
they would ”look for a heart icon,” which in retrospect, we feel may have been influenced
by a sketch we had just previously shown them thereby priming them to think this way
(note the heart-shaped ”save” icons in the shared shelf sketch in Figure 5.3).
In regards to evaluating books, participants liked the idea of rating different aspects of a
book and suggested that the system provide a limited number of (secondary) parameters
that the user can select depending on their interests and book relevance. We also dis-
cussed that in order to maintain some level of consistency among all users, there should
be one default overall rating that can be used to quickly assess books and would be
shared among everyone. We agreed that this common (primary) rating should be clearly
visible at the top of the Book Info page while the secondary rating parameters could be
listed further down on the page.
Rather than displaying a predicted rating based on the user’s interests, which might con-
fuse users already managing various types of ratings, the participants suggested we follow
the example set by Netflix, in which we report a percentage match between the book and
the user’s tastes (e.g., ”This book is an 84% match with your interests”).
Participants were enthusiastic about the idea to include review prompts, which would
help users structure their thoughts to better reflect on a book. They also mentioned that
enabling users to have control over the privacy of each of their published reviews would
be ideal.
A more exhaustive report of the findings and discussion from the first testing can be found
in Appendix K.
These findings helped us to create an initial User Story Map, which allowed us to map out
the activities and tasks users would need to accomplish in order to meet their goals when
using the book-tracking system (a shortened version can be seen in Figure 5.4). Some of
the more important activities we included in our USM include saving a book for later and
managing shared book collections. Saving a book for later can be done both ”quickly”

(using a shortcut from the Discover page) or ”slowly” (going through the Book Info page
and having the opportunity to write a quick-note while adding). Moving forward from this
stage, we wanted to explore how these flows could look with some considerations in mind,
such as how to prevent the quick-add method from being too unclear or hidden and how to
make sure the ”slow-add” method doesn’t require too many taps, which could get irritating
for the user over time. With managing shared book collections, we really had to envision
what this could look like in terms of adding books to the shelf, writing the recommendation
note, adding collaborators, etc. Another important activity we outlined was evaluating the
book, which could look a variety of ways. While we wanted to augment the review process
with the additional ratings and review prompts, we also wanted to be cautious about not
making the process too complicated, as this would likely deter users from wanting to write
reviews at all. To see our expanded USM for this iteration and all subsequent iterations,
refer to Appendix M.
5.4
Iteration 2
5.4.1
What was tested
The second iteration, which was also our first interactive Figma prototype, focused on
the general experience of managing collections and evaluating books. However, we also
performed four more specific additional tests at this stage, which focused on:
• the flow of marking a book as ”read” and then subsequently accessing (or skipping)
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
65
==PAGE:72==
the review process,
• the potential ways in which the review prompt feature could look and be used,
• how to provide appropriate feedback to the user to communicate that a book has
been successfully quick-added to the user’s ”Want to read” shelf from the Discover
page,
• UI design.
As per the first item, we played around with the idea of whether the user should be au-
tomatically navigated to rate the book and write a review immediately after marking it as
”read”. Figure 5.5 displays another option, which would be to provide a prompt in which
the user is asked whether they want to rate and write a review with the option to skip the
action (and return to it later). The latter incorporates more taps in the flow, which is not
usually preferable, but may be necessary in order to give the user more control over the
sequence of actions (not all users have the desire or time to rate and write a review when
they mark it as ”read”).
Figure 5.5: Prompt shown to the user upon marking the book as ”read”
We had two different visions of what the review prompt feature could look like on the Rate
This Book page. One option was to include a button in which users would be redirected
to an inventory of prompts that they could then scroll through and select from in order
to have the chosen prompt appear back on the Rate This Book page as they write their
response (see Figure 5.6a and Figure 5.6b). Another option was to create a carousel of
review prompts situated directly on the Rate This Book page as illustrated in Figure 5.6c,
which users could then tap through to view other prompt options.
Providing appropriate feedback after quick-adding a book from the Discover page had
66
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:73==
(a) Button to show prompts
list
(b) Prompts displayed in a list
(c) Prompts displayed in a
carousel
Figure 5.6: Different variants of showing review prompts in the second iteration
more to do with the microinteraction of how and where the notification appears on the
screen rather than the contents or design of the notification itself. Figure 5.7a shows an
example in which the notification appears at the top of the screen, regardless of which
book was saved. Figure 5.7b, on the other hand, shows the notification appearing on top
of the saved book entry. While this change may seem trivial, we were interested in seeing
if the impact on the user was equal or if one communicated the action more clearly than
the other.
As it was our first non-sketched prototype, we were also interested in the general response
to the visuals - color scheme, clarity, and overall aesthetics. We didn’t strictly follow any
specific guidelines, but we tried to keep the UI minimalist, use commonly recognizable
icons, and stick to standard iOS controls.
5.4.2
How it was tested
The second phase of testing consisted of a thinking-aloud test, A/B testing, and a UI at-
tractiveness rating. We also invited two users to participate together. We felt that because
we were still early on in the prototyping process, having two users comfortable with one
another will allow them to build off one another and potentially help us co-create through
providing feedback and ideating.
For the thinking-aloud test, we asked that the participants essentially explore the features
and screens on their own while voicing their thoughts, opinions, and criticisms along the
way. In the Library page, we specifically mentioned that the purpose would be for the
user to manage their collections. On the Discover page, we instructed that the goal was
to save a book to a shelf and ultimately access the rating and reviewing process.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
67
==PAGE:74==
(a) Sticky notification on the top
of the page
(b) Notification centered on the
affected book
Figure 5.7: Different placements of the notification shown after quick-adding a book on
the Discover page
We then performed three A/B tests matching the items delineated above (i.e., the flow
of marking a book as ”read” and then accessing the review process; the review prompt
feature design; and the quick-add feedback on the Discover page).
Finally, we discussed the overall aesthetics and UI of the wireframes and asked the par-
ticipants to assign the UI a rating from 1-10 (10 being the best).
5.4.3
Outcome of the tests
The two participants from this testing were most closely aligned with the BookTok reader
archetype and the Power reader archetype. Both indicated that they actively use Goodreads,
but they do not enjoy it, which was unknown to us prior to testing.
A summary of their comments from the general thinking-aloud test is as follows:
• the ”Did not finish” shelf is great because Goodreads does not support it, and one of
the test participants often finds herself abandoning books she started with no clear
way to document this,
• the quick-notes feature in the ”Want to read” shelf was well-liked as a way to provide
context or to write notes while reading,
• the shared shelf was considered great for recommending books to friends,
• the participants found the overall look of the wireframes to be much more preferable
than the look of Goodreads,
68
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:75==
• the ability to rate different elements of the book was appreciated, as was the ability
for users to specify half-stars when rating a book (also not supported in Goodreads),
• at the same time, the additional ratings should be optional when submitting the re-
view,
• the review prompts would be good for helping users reflect more on the book,
• upon marking the book as ”read” and being redirected to the Rate This Book page
(the default flow we configured), the participants noted that it would be nice if they
could skip the rating and reviewing in the case in which they just want to log the
book as done,
• it would be nice if there was a simple way to add the book to a ”favorites” list after
reading.
As per the first A/B test we conducted, the participants preferred the flow in which the
user is prompted with a question of whether they want to rate and review the book (rather
than immediately redirecting them to the Rate This Book page). They shared that they
don’t always have time to review a book but like the satisfaction of marking it as done.
Regarding the two review prompt versions, the participants suggested we combine the
ideas. We discussed how users could access the inventory of prompts and after selecting
one, they could browse additional prompts using either arrow button on the Rate This Book
page version. The feedback about the two different quick-add notification placements was
inconclusive, as both participants preferred different versions. This suggested to us that
either choice would ultimately be acceptable.
The UI rating agreed upon between the participants was 7.5. They expressed that it
was minimalist and intuitive. When comparing to Goodreads, they said it was simpler
and easier to read but could be more distinguishable as it seems to have a somewhat
similar color scheme and aesthetic. This was understandable as we are familiar with the
Goodreads app after actively using it for over a year, making us biased and primed as
designers.
At this point, we updated the USM, as displayed in Figure 5.8 to separate the activities of
the quick-add and ”slow-add” methods, as highlighted by the stars in the image. We also
added an activity regarding managing the user’s library, which includes a unique set of
tasks compared to those related to managing the user’s shelves. The fourth key change
to the USM involved further developing the shared shelves to be interactive among collab-
orators, as specified by the starred task stating, ”like and comment on recommendations
from shared shelf collaborators.”
5.5
Iteration 3
5.5.1
What was tested
In the third iteration, we focused on incorporating comments from the previous round of
testing:
• changed the color scheme to blue to differentiate from Goodreads,
• added positive feedback upon marking a book as ”read”,
• added an option to mark a book as ”Favorite” using a heart icon,
• added opening the review prompt list by tapping the carousel item

and tested different ways of showing current book status (e.g., ”Want to read” or ”Did not
finish”) on the Book Info page.
To add positive feedback for completing books, we experimented with multiple ideas. One
of which followed a gamification approach known from gaming platforms like Steam - the
user was presented with various achievement badges after marking the book as ”read”
(Figure 5.9a). Another approach mimicked the popular Goodreads ”Reading Challenge”
feature, which allows users to check their reading progress on their profile. Our adapted
version of this feedback method would appear as a pop-up once a book is marked as
”read” (Figure 5.9b). As we were not sure which set of information might be the most
interesting or important to the user, we prepared three different variants of the notification
- showing only the badges, showing only the progress bar, and showing both the badges
and the progress bar (Figure 5.9c).
When it comes to showing the current book status on the Book Info page, we had different
ideas using icons (Figure 5.10a, Figure 5.10b) and simple textual labels on the book
cover (Figure 5.10c). It was important for us to determine through testing which of these
strategies would be the most clear and easy to understand for users.
5.5.2
How it was tested
The third round of testing was done with two participants, each on different days. Similarly
to the previous round of validation, we conducted a thinking-aloud test, followed by two
A/B tests. However, unlike the previous round in which we felt the thinking-aloud was
somewhat chaotic and unstructured, this time we prepared a task list to ensure that users
70
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:77==
(a) Achievement badges
displayed in a list
(b) Progress bar
(c) Badges and progress bar
combined
Figure 5.9: Different variants of positive feedback upon finishing the book
explored all features and functionalities (see Appendix L), which was followed by some
free time for participants to explore the wireframes on their own. We then discussed
overall thoughts, opinions, and criticisms. After this, we performed the two A/B tests,
which both focused on forms of feedback provided to the user after marking a book as
”read”. Finally, we asked participants to rate the UI overall and explain the thought process
behind their choice.
5.5.3
Outcome of the tests
One of the participants from this round of testing could be classified as a Print-only reader,
while the other was an even mix of the BookTok and Self-help archetypes. Neither of them
had used a book-tracking app before, which was evident during the thinking-aloud test in
which they were unfamiliar with typical book-tracking concepts. This was especially useful
for us because it so happened that one of the major themes from this round of feedback
was clarity. From our perspective as designers, a strong user experience would need to
be clear not only to book-tracking users from other apps but also to non-users who are
just trying out book-tracking for the first time.
In the Library view, it was unclear to the participants that the shelves were clickable rather
than the individual books (see Figure 5.11a. We believe this could’ve been because the
shelf titles seemed secondary to the actual book covers, which were much more notice-
able and horizontally scrollable. The plus icon in the top left corner of the page, which is
meant to convey ”add shelf,” was understandable to previous test participants who were
familiar with book-tracking. However, in this testing round, the function was less clear
to participants, which suggested to us that adding a text label would help to clarify its
purpose and remove the need for new users to guess. Additionally, the search feature
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
71
==PAGE:78==
(a) Icon displayed next to the
button
(b) Icon displayed on top of
the book cover
(c) Text label displayed on the
book cover
Figure 5.10: Different variants of displaying current reading stages. All the books are in
the ”Want to read” stage.
(floating at the bottom of the page in Figure 5.11b) was confusing to participants both in
terms of visibility and functionality. For example, they were unsure if users could search
for their shelves, books, etc.
Again, the shared shelf was a popular concept, but it also seemed to be another point
of confusion for participants. For one, the group icon next to the shared shelf name on
the Library page (see Figure 5.11c) was not easily noticeable, making the shared shelf
difficult to discern among the other shelves. The naming of the shelf (”Østerbro Hus”) also
contributed to this issue, which we later learned was because they were looking for the
name ”Shared Shelf” rather than a customized name as we had chosen. Once inside the
shelf, there were also some questions about how to see the number of collaborators in
the shared shelf, which we agreed would be important information to provide to the user.
(a) Shelf visible in the Library
(b) Search button at the
bottom of the screen
(c) Shared shelf with its icon
Figure 5.11: Problematic elements of the Library page in the third iteration.
On the Book Info page, we learned that when asking users to locate the book’s ”pace”
rating (an example of a secondary rating parameter), most of them would instinctively
tap the overall book rating at the top of the page, which was not interactive at the time.
There was also confusion about the ”slow-add” process (adding a book to a shelf from
the Book Info page). Participants were able to easily understand how to add the book to
the ”Want to read” shelf using the button labeled ”Add to shelf.” However, when we later
asked them to mark the book as ”read” (past tense), which would be accessible through
the same ”Add to shelf” button, participants were unsure how to proceed. We believe
this issue was caused by two main factors - the first being that the users were unfamiliar
with typical book-tracking concepts, which often classify books into collections or shelves
which correlate to a stage of reading (i.e., ”Want to read”, ”Currently reading”, or ”Read”).
72
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:79==
The second reason we suspected was the phrasing of the task during testing, which said
”mark the book as ’read’”. This was likely misleading to users, especially after the previous
similar task was worded as, ”add the book to your ’Want to read’ shelf”.
This finding highlighted a key detail for us that we had previously overlooked, which was
that there are often two main use case scenarios in which users would be marking a book
as ”read”. In one scenario, the user could be moving a book from ”Currently reading”
to ”Read”. In another scenario, the user could immediately mark a book as ”read” when
using the app for the first time and logging books they had previously read in the past.
This learning prompted us to start to rethink the process of adding books to shelves in
order to make the process clear for both established and new users alike.
Some of the other aspects that were unclear to participants include:
• quick-notes in the ”Want to read” shelf (unclear whether they are filled or un-filled),
which can be seen in Figure 5.12,
• how to insert a half-star when rating a book (the intention was for a full star to appear
upon the user’s first tap and then be replaced by a half-star upon the second tap),
• the ”add to favorites” button at the bottom of the Rate This Book page (some par-
ticipants were unsure if it applied to the book or the review),
• how to use the review prompts feature (participants were unsure if it would be re-
quired to use or if the prompt would be published along with the review).
Figure 5.12: Books with and without quick-notes on the ”Want to read” shelf
As for the A/B tests, we found that both participants preferred the version in which text
banners were placed over the book cover to denote which shelf it’s in, as displayed in
Figure 5.10c. Regarding the positive feedback pop-up when marking a book as ”read”,
the participants had differing views; one preferred the version in which the achievement
badges and reading goal progress were combined (as in Figure 5.9c), while the other liked
the components individually but felt the combined version was too much information at
once. Regardless, our conclusion was that it would be safer to implement the combined
version for now and explore more tailored options at a later point in which users could
potentially control the level of feedback they receive.
Overall, we received positive feedback regarding the review prompts and shared shelf
concepts. The UI ratings for this iteration were an 8 and 9, respectively, but it’s important
to keep in mind that these users were not familiar with other book-tracking apps. To read
a detailed account of the testing and feedback for the third iteration, refer to Appendix K.
At the end of the third iteration, the main difference made to the USM involved the order
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking

reflect the fusion between the review prompt library and the review carousel. Besides this
change, most of the feedback we received during this iteration reflected issues of clarity,
as previously discussed.
