Section 2
Figure 5.13: User Story Map iteration 3
5.6
Iteration 4
5.6.1
What was tested
After the previous iteration, we decided to focus on two major aspects in the fourth round
of testing:
• improving Library view to clarify that tapping a shelf label opens the Shelf view
instead of individual books,
• changing the way of adding books to shelves, so it doesn’t require prior knowledge
of book-tracking concepts and is understandable for new users.
We developed several ideas for a new way of moving books between shelves. The first
one included adding a dynamic button that explicitly states what happens to the book
upon clicking it and changes according to the current book state (see Figure 5.14a and
Figure 5.14b). For example, when viewing a new book the button says ”Want to read” to
indicate that the book would be added to the ”Want to read” shelf after tapping it. After
being tapped, the button transforms to say ”Now reading” to inform that the book will be
moved to the ”Currently reading” shelf.
Another idea was a simple dropdown menu labeled ”Status”, which allows the user to
move the book to a different shelf depending on the book’s status, as demonstrated in
74
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:81==
Figure 5.14c. The third and final variant displayed a horizontal slider with all the available
reading stages (see Figure 5.14d). The user would simply drag the slider to the desired
stage to change the book’s designated shelf.
While the first solution look was the simplest, it was not as clear and flexible as the drop-
down and slider which allowed the user to see all the possible options and freely jump
between them. Also, although the slider nicely displayed all the stages and was more
visual than the dropdown menu, this type of control is typically used to express range in-
put instead of the stage/status of the system, and because of this, incorporating the ”Did
not finish” shelf was challenging. Thus, one of our goals during this stage of testing was
to determine which of these three variants was the most appealing and the simplest to
understand for users.
Additionally, we implemented various smaller changes mentioned by the test participants.
Those include:
• shared shelf improvements, like changing its name and showing information about
the number of collaborators,
• adding or completely replacing icons with text labels where possible, e.g., for quick-
notes and creating new shelves,
• changing star behavior from being fully filled after the first tap and half filled after the
second tap to half-fill on the first tap and full-fill on the second tap (Figure 5.15)
• adding/clarifying various help labels in different places, e.g., related to ”Favorites”
or review prompts,
• adding scrolling to different rating parameters upon tapping the overall rating.
As with every iteration, we continued investigating the UI appeal and general application
flow. We stuck to the blue theme, but this time changed the font across the whole prototype
and added an additional one which we used only for book titles.
5.6.2
How it was tested
The fourth round of validation involved three participants total, two of which tested to-
gether. With everyone, we oversaw another thinking-aloud test, followed by two A/B tests,
one of which pertained to the process of adding a book to a default shelf, for which we
had three working versions, as described in detail above.
The second A/B test focused more on the UI of the Library view and communicating the
user’s assortment of shelves as opposed to drawing more attention to the books. We
showed participants six versions, which can be found in Figure 5.16, and then we asked
them to choose and defend their top preference.
After a final discussion about the system overall and what worked for participants com-
pared to what didn’t, we again asked them to ascribe an overall UI rating to the system
thus far.
Separately, we performed a type of empathy exercise as designers in which we attempted
to adopt the point of view of the reader archetypes who had not yet been properly repre-
sented in our four iterations of testing. In our case, we identified the Novice and Self-help
archetypes to be the most under-represented. In this activity, we laid out the main char-
acteristics of each archetype and analyzed the prototype page by page, commenting on
and identifying elements that may be useful or challenging to use for said reader type.
We will refer to them as Point-of-View (POV) tests.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
75
==PAGE:82==
5.6.3
Outcome of the tests
Out of three participants, two of them were Power Readers, one was half BookTok, half
Print-only. When it comes to book-tracking experience, one of them was book-tracking
manually using Notion, the other one was using automatic Kindle tracking and the last
one was not tracking her reading at all.
We had two major points of interest during this iteration - picking one of six variants of the
Library view and the most intuitive and clear way of changing the book reading stage. In
the case of the Library view, users liked variants A, B, and E and the default one. The dif-
ferences between them were purely visual, not affecting functionality, therefore the users
explained their picks simply by saying that they were more ”aesthetically pleasing” or ”felt
more professional”. However, changing the reading stage sparked a bit more discussion,
as different variants had both different looks and functionality. Variant A (”dynamic but-
ton”) was considered fine, but only for the case when the user actively tracks the book in
the app and goes through all the stages there; logging past books required several un-
necessary clicks. All the users, on the other hand, valued the ability to see all the options
in the dropdown (both the default and new one) and the slider. However, they slightly
preferred the dropdown approach due to its simplicity and ease of use.
During that testing round, we had several interesting observations regarding the wording
and clarity of different terms. It turned out that users had problems understanding what the
”Read” shelf is due to the English language ambiguity - both present and past tense are
written the same way which led to misunderstandings. Also, one of the users understood
”Did not finish” as ”In progress” which was understandable after being pointed out but did
not occur to us while designing.
Another set of comments regarded shared shelves. First of all, the ”x” icon to remove a
book from the ”Want to read” shelf was unclear - users commented that it was not clear
whether it removes the book from the ”Want to read” shelf or from the shared shelf. Also,
they said that functionality of both the ”+” and ”x” icons were not immediately clear and
required tapping them to actually see what happens. On top of that, they commented that
tapping the ”Like” button under the recommendation should change it in a more visible
way than only swapping text from ”Like” to ”Liked”.
Part of the feedback concerned the Rate This Book page. One of the users was a bit
confused by rating parameters and the way they are assigned - she understood the overall
rating as an average automatically calculated from the partial ratings. Also, there was a
suggestion to divide rating parameters based on e.g., book type (fiction/non-fiction) or
platform (e-book/audiobook).
Other comments during this iteration were related to changing the quick-note icon on the
Book Info page and adding an option to create a new shelf while adding a book to a custom
shelf.
On the other hand, we received positive feedback about the review prompts and their
current implementation, new Library view, clarity of the updated quick-notes, and intu-
itiveness of shortcut scrolling to partial book ratings by tapping the overall rating on the
Book Info page.
Novice and Self-help POV tests
When it comes to POV tests, we started by looking at the app from the perspective of a po-
tential Novice reader. We found several app aspects which might be especially important
for this type of user:
76
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:83==
• privacy of the shelves should be clearly visible and modifiable, as they might not
want to show all of their activity at the beginning,
• it’s important to help them easily find generally likable books, so they should be able
to sort their saved books by popularity,
• words and terms in the app should be clear and unambiguous, as that type of users
are probably not familiar with book-tracking ”jargon”
• seeing progress over time and being given positive feedback is important to keep
the motivation up for users who only recently started reading more,
• interface should be as simple as possible, without too many unnecessary features
visible by default, so the user does not feel overwhelmed when opening the app for
the first time.
Another archetype whose point of view we examined was the Self-help reader. Our at-
tention was mostly drawn to the following aspects:
• grouping of rating parameters might be helpful for them, as they will likely be inter-
ested in different book aspects than fiction readers,
• as active readers, they might use quick-notes a lot, so they might want to see them
when writing a review,
• also, they might want to write a review while reading a book, so review drafts might
be important for them,
• they might group books in the Library according to their professions and hobbies
(e.g., ”gardening”, ”painting”), so they might benefit from in-shelf recommendations
which would suggest a book matching the current shelf’s theme.
At the end of the fourth iteration, our goal was to create one final prototype before pausing
the cycle. With this, we wanted to incorporate as many of the comments and suggestions
we received in the fourth round of testing as possible. To reflect this, the USM expanded
vertically greatly, as visible in Figure 5.17, which shows the ”manage personal library”
activity including various new tasks regarding sorting and filtering, which we felt would
be important in a ”final” solution. Additionally, we wanted to finally add a way to view the
shared shelf collaborators, as it had been included on the USM for every iteration but had
not been developed yet.
5.7
Discussion
One of the major trade-offs we had to make during this phase of the design process was
choosing between testing with more people or developing more iterations. Ideally, we
would have liked to conduct at least five different tests for each iteration in order to obtain
a substantial amount of feedback to consider moving forward. Ultimately, we tested with
only two to three users per prototype (until the final iteration), which therefore limited us in
the quality of the overall feedback. However, this afforded us more time to refine the Figma
prototype during each iteration and experiment with more aspects of the app, resulting in
five total iterations. Taking into account the difficulties with organizing a workshop early
on, we also expected difficulties in finding volunteers for prototype tests. This convinced
us even further to prioritize the number of iterations over the number of user tests.
Within our iterations, we also struggled to incorporate more quantitative validation meth-
ods aside from the UI ratings. Doing more quantitative tests would have given us more
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
77
==PAGE:84==
objective data to clearly analyze and harness in arguing for or against certain changes.
Instead, we relied heavily on qualitative feedback, which was always subjective and re-
quired interpretation to make conclusions. During the third iteration, we attempted to
perform an error-counting test while the user carried out the various tasks we described.
This was unsuccessful and felt futile because the Figma prototype was often not properly
registering the users’ taps. Upon further reflection, while it would have been beneficial
to conduct more quantitative tests, we also feel that the approach we took in testing with
only two to three users per iteration would severely limit the value of that quantitative
data, which would be more effective coming from a larger sample size of users. The
UI ratings, for example, would have been particularly helpful in the event in which there
was a drastic shift in rating between two iterations, which would’ve indicated to us that
we had made some considerable upgrade or downgrade to the design overall. However,
because the ratings we received were all in the same range of 7-9, we felt that this test
was unremarkable and somewhat inconclusive.
Additionally, we also recognized the importance of preparing task lists, which would guide
users to explore relevant features during the thinking-aloud tests. Without them, we had
to periodically inform participants of certain functionalities they had missed. This could
indicate that some features were not as clear or intuitive to use, but in the context of this
prototype, we believe it was also partly because we were using Figma and there were
many pieces of the wireframes that were not interactive. This made it difficult for users to
know what they can tap and explore during testing as opposed to what is purely a visual
element of the prototype.
Another element of the prototyping phase that we found noteworthy was the division of
participants who use book-tracking versus those who were unfamiliar with it. We inten-
tionally wanted to include both types because, ideally, a book-tracking system with an im-
proved user experience would be intuitive to users from other platforms as well as users
completely new to book-tracking. The insights between these user types can be charac-
terized by some general differences. For one, people who don’t use book-tracking may
have less knowledge of the typical concepts used, such as shelves and ratings. They’re
especially useful for testing how clear and usable a system is because they don’t bring in
much prior knowledge or ideas of what everything should look like or how it should work.
On the other hand, users who already use book-tracking may have certain expectations
and biases, which influence their opinions. It’s still important that we try to meet these
minimum expectations because they might dictate whether or not a user would switch
platforms or not.
Overall, we feel that using Figma both had its advantages and disadvantages. The pro-
gram allowed us to prototype a system that looked similar to real applications and was
interactive to an extent. However, there were multiple instances in which Figma would
crash or components would disconnect from their original implementation. That ultimately
forced us to spend a substantial amount of time correcting issues or re-implementing cer-
tain elements.

Section 1
Figure 5.17: User Story Map iteration 4
82
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:89==
6
Final Iteration & Evaluation
6.1
Introduction
In this chapter, we present the key parts of our final prototype, together with the last round
of validation, and more detailed comments about all the elements we managed to create
in relation to our initial problem statement.
6.2
What was tested - final prototype presentation
Our final prototype consists of four main screens - the Library and Shelf page, the Book
Info page, and the Rate This Book page. The Library, Shelf, and Rate This Book pages
were strictly required to tackle issues related to managing collections and evaluating
books we discovered during our user research. While the Book Info page contains a
number of elements outside the scope (e.g., the book summary, tags) it also serves as a
critical component for performing tasks within both focus areas, such as adding a book to
a shelf or accessing the Rate This Book page. The top parts of the pages can be seen in
Figure 6.1.
6.2.1
Managing collections
In our case, collections management consists of various smaller activities, e.g., managing
the whole library and its shelves, adding/removing books to specific shelves (with optional
additional context information), or managing shared collections. All of them include dif-
ferent, smaller tasks to accomplish and provide value to the user. To see the whole User
Story Map which guided us for the last iteration, refer to Appendix M.
When it comes to library management, our prototype allows its users to perform seven
tasks, starting from browsing all the available shelves and adding new ones to searching
all the books by title or author name. The whole library consists of four default shelves
(”Currently reading”, ”Want to read”, ”Finished”, and ”Did not finish”) and additional custom
shelves created by the user. All the shelves present their books as horizontally scrollable
lists. The ”Currently reading” card allows users to quickly perform the most common
action on the books in this reading stage, which is logging progress and marking them as
”finished”. All of the relevant interface parts can be seen in Figure 6.1a.
Once users tap on a shelf in the Library view, they enter the collection. In this view, at
first glance, the user can see all of their books presented in a vertical list. Every book
entry includes the book cover, title, author, rating information, and a quick-note. Users
can sort the books by several predefined criteria (e.g., title or author), search them, and
filter by e.g., genres or tags (Figure 6.2a). At the top of the page, the user is shown a book
recommendation which is based on the shelf contents. By default, shelves are displayed
as a list, but there is also an option to view them as a grid of book covers (Figure 6.2b).
As the ability to control privacy appeared frequently in our user research and is not well-
supported in the existing book-tracking platforms, it’s also possible to change this aspect
of the shelf at the top of the screen. Another commonly occurring theme, which is novel
to our solution, was the concept of context information or quick-notes about the books.
These are visible and editable directly from the Shelf view, as seen in Figure 6.2c.
A special type of shelf we have implemented, which none of the competitors have, is a
shared shelf, which allows users to see and edit shelves together. This shelf looks similar
to others in terms of book presentation, but instead of displaying the rating and the private
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
83
==PAGE:90==
quick-note, it includes a recommendation note provided by the user who added the book
to the shared shelf, and a button to quickly add it to one’s personal ”Want to read” shelf
(Figure 6.3a). Collaborators can also ”Like” the book or discuss it in the comments. The
shelf owner can add and remove collaborators using a simple modal (Figure 6.3b).
Moving books between shelves is the last important part of collection management. In
our prototype, the most common operations of this type are performed directly from the
shelves and the rest can be done from the Book Info page. Using a status dropdown,
the user can move the book between the four default shelves (”Want to read”, ”Currently
reading”, ”Finished”, and ”Did not finish”) (Figure 6.4a). As seen in Figure 6.4b, when the
default shelf is changed, it is also indicated by a different book label and outline color of
the card. Below the status dropdown, there is a collapsible section showing the user’s
custom shelves together with a button opening a modal used to move the book between
them (Figure 6.4c). On the Book Info page, the user can also change book format (writ-
ten/audiobook), access their quick-note, and add the book to their ”Favorites” shelf using
the icons on the right side of the header.
6.2.2
Evaluating books
The second area we focused on during our design phase was the process of evaluating
books after finishing them. We wanted to make it easy for the users to accurately express
their views and simplify the process of writing a review to encourage more users to add
them.
To rate a book, the user first needs to mark it as ”finished”. Then, the Book Info page
displays a button that brings the user to the Rate This Book page (Figure 6.5). This page
can be divided into two major sections - the top consists of the interactive star ratings
and the bottom displays a text box used to input the user’s review, as well as the review’s
privacy settings.
As plenty of complaints during the user research pertained to the users’ inability to ex-
press opinions more precisely in Goodreads, we implemented the concept of secondary
rating parameters. As is done in StoryGraph, these rating parameters focus on sepa-
rate aspects of the book the user can rate independently from the overall rating. Unlike
in StoryGraph, however, with our solution users have control over deciding which of the
parameters they deem relevant for the given book and can select them through a ded-
icated menu (Figure 6.6a). The only required, common rating is the overall one. Also,
to allow users to express their ratings more precisely than Goodreads currently allows,
although there are only five stars visible, users can also rate using half-stars making the
scale effectively have ten steps (Figure 6.6b).
When it comes to the book reviews, users mentioned multiple issues with the varied quality
of published reviews and the lack of structure and control of the open-ended reviews
of Goodreads. In the end, we focused on helping them structure their thoughts while
writing, which should translate into more reviews of better quality. Instead of guiding users
through a lengthy step-by-step process like in StoryGraph, we help them by providing
optional review prompts, which users can access using the button right above the text
field. These questions allow users to better articulate their thoughts while writing their
review (Figure 6.7a). After tapping a question, users are redirected back to the previous
page where the prompt is then displayed above the text field (Figure 6.7b). A prompt can
be easily swapped with others by tapping the arrows on either side of it. To make it easier
for users to recall their thoughts, they can access their quick-notes or write the review
while reading the book and consistently save it as a draft (Figure 6.7c). As the privacy
of the review was another commonly occurring theme, we added a dropdown that allows
84
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:91==
users to select who will be able to see the published review (Figure 6.7c).
The Book Info page includes both the primary and secondary ratings as well as the reviews
of the book. At the top of the page, the average overall rating is displayed (Figure 6.5a).
Upon tapping this general rating, the view scrolls down to the secondary rating parameters
where users can see the average ratings of different book aspects (Figure 6.8a). They
can also select which parameters they want to see using the same menu as the one used
on the Rate This Book page (Figure 6.6a). At the bottom of the page, there is a list of
reviews submitted by other readers (Figure 6.8b), which can be ”liked” or commented
on. When the user submits their own rating and review, the page slightly changes - the
user’s rating is displayed at the top, just below the status dropdown, and their review is
displayed at the top of the review section (Figure 6.8c, Figure 6.8d). Both can be edited
at any moment in time.
Links to the interactive final prototype and map of micro-interactions can be found in Ap-
pendix N and Appendix O.
6.3
How was it tested
For the final iteration, we tested with five users independently, so as to gather as many
final comments as possible to better understand how well our solution confronts the de-
fined problem. Three of the five users had participated in user testing with us during an
earlier iteration and were therefore familiar with the project while the other two were learn-
ing about it for the first time. We followed a similar framework as our previous iterations
by again performing a thinking-aloud test with each user. After this, we asked each user
to complete the NASA Task Load Index (NASA TLX)[42] survey followed by the System
Usability Scale (SUS)[43] survey. To simplify the process, we skipped the pairwise com-
parison step of NASA TLX and just asked the users to complete the survey. We also
created our own survey, as depicted in Figure 6.9, which attempts to refer back to the
major elements of our problem statement. We ended each user test by having one final
discussion about the system overall to receive each user’s final thoughts.
For our final evaluation, we wanted to then supplement the user testing by doing our own
round of analytical testing, critically comparing our system to the guidelines set forth by
Jakob Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics for User Interface Design [41].
6.4
Outcome of the tests
We will describe the outcome of the final testing in two sections based on the main focus
areas of our design process. Also, compared to the previous iterations, we will include
possible improvements while describing the flaws found during testing.
6.4.1
Managing collections
The first tested area related to collections management was the main Library view. In
general, users were happy with its aesthetics and functionality. In the final version, we
managed to get rid of the problem with the ambiguous naming of the ”Read” shelf by
renaming it to ”Finished”. One of the main issues we were struggling with throughout the
whole design process was wording and naming various app aspects. The only comments
regarding this part of the design pertained to the ease of closing the feedback modal after
marking the book as ”finished” and the position of the ”Currently reading” shelf. In the last
version, the modal could be closed by pressing anywhere outside it which was not clear
for one user who preferred having a dedicated button to do it. According to another user,
the position of the ”Currently reading” shelf was too much at the top of the page which
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
85
==PAGE:92==
made them consider it an advertisement at the beginning and suggested putting this shelf
more in the middle of the screen.
When testing the Shelf view (using the ”Want to read” shelf as an example), users were
particularly fond of the book recommendation feature. They found it easy to understand,
but one user suggested adding a way of going back to the previous recommendation and
another proposed changing colors, as the current ones made it look like an advertisement
for them. Another functionality that did not cause any issues or misunderstandings was
privacy - all of the users easily found the current shelf privacy settings and were able to
change them. A feature particularly interesting to test was removing a book from a shelf
by swiping. We had not clearly indicated that it was possible and relied heavily on users’
intuition derived from the usage of other apps having this functionality. To our positive
surprise, three out of five users intuitively performed the required gesture and said that it
was ”like Instagram or email”. The other two were looking for an explicit icon or tried long-
pressing the book to open a context menu. This also made us think about adding an option
to multi-select books after long-pressing, as that is another common functionality in apps
like email or messages. The most confusing feature on this page was filtering. While the
filtering sheet itself was intuitive to use, users had problems with finding an icon to open
it. Nearly all of them said they were looking for the ”Filter” option somewhere around the
”Sorted by” label, not next to the search bar at the bottom of the page. Grouping these
three elements likely makes sense, as they contribute to the same goal of finding books
and, according to the fourth Guiding Principle for Experience Design by Whitney Hess1,
they should be kept together.
Throughout all the iterations, the shared shelves idea was met with enthusiasm, but we
were consistently struggling with making them easily identifiable among the other shelves
in the library. In the last iteration, the shelf had a dedicated icon next to its name which
opened an information modal upon tapping (Figure 6.10a and Figure 6.10b). It helped
some of the users, while others were still unsure how to locate it. We suspect it might
also be related to the phrasing of the task we were giving and users looking for a shelf
named literally ”Shared”. Using the shared shelf itself was looking much better. When
asked to add the top book from the shared to their ”Want to read” collections, none of
the users had problems. When asked to add collaborators to the book, some users first
clicked the ”Info” icon, but when it did not allow them to perform the required action, they
were opening the correct modal (Figure 6.10c). If clicking the ”Info” icon would explain
how to add users, it might make the experience even smoother.
The final design of the Book Info page in terms of collection management was mostly fine
and users usually pointed out rather minor issues. The biggest one revolved around the
dropdown to change the reading status of the book. When asked to move the book to
their ”Want to read” list, for one user it did not occur to them that it could be done through
the dropdown labeled ”Status”, but later they said it made perfect sense after the first
usage. Another user commented that the dropdown did not draw enough attention taking
into account how key functionality it represents. On the other hand, none of the users had
problems using the dropdown itself and changing the book reading stages once they found
it for the first time. Therefore, although it is not a perfect solution and might be improved
by e.g., making the dropdown more prominent, it is also not abnormal that users need
to learn some aspects of using an app. When it comes to the custom shelves, all users
considered it simple to find the shelves the book is currently added to. Removing the book
1https://whitneyhess.com/blog/2009/11/23/so-you-wanna-be-a-user-experience-designer-step-2-guiding-
principles/ (accessed: 2023-06-15)
86
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:93==
from the custom shelf was a bit more confusing, as the modal to add and remove a book
from different shelves was hidden under ”Add to custom shelf”. That issue, however, can
be easily mitigated by changing the label of the button to ”Edit custom shelves” or similar,
which makes it clearer that it allows for both addition and removal. After changing the
icon of quick-notes, the task to view them on the Book Info page ceased to be a problem
for all of the testers. Additionally, we rephrased the task and separated it from moving the
book to the ”Want to read” shelf which probably also made it simpler to understand. All
of the participants also successfully changed the book format to an audiobook, yet some
had reservations about the icon itself, e.g., mentioned that it was not clear enough and
did not clearly depict the different formats.
6.4.2
Evaluating books
While targeting issues with evaluating books, we focused on the two most important as-
pects - assigning a book a quantitative rating using stars and a qualitative rating using
reviews.
To give a star rating, users had to tap the star once (half-filled) or twice (fully-filled). None
of them had any problems understanding this concept, yet some suggested that it would
be more convenient to drag to fill the stars (like a slider). Although we got this feedback
also in the previous iterations, we were not able to implement it due to Figma limitations.
However, it is definitely worth noting, as that suggestion appeared multiple times. When
it comes to rating parameters, which allow the users to rate different aspects of the book,
the whole idea was generally well-received. Only one user had issues understanding how
they work - they assumed that they had to input the secondary ratings and the overall
rating would be automatically calculated as an average of the others. This issue can
probably be easily solved by adding an ”Info” icon and modal which briefly explain the
concept of rating parameters. The menu in which users can select rating parameters
that are relevant to them was also clear and understandable for all. One user noted the
usefulness of the info button for added clarity. Another user was not sure where the search
bar applies, but that can be mitigated by improving the wording (e.g. changing ”Search
for parameters” to ”Search all parameters”).
Our proposal of the review prompts to guide users while writing was also met with positive
feedback. No one had problems understanding how to use them and what they do. All of
the users performed tasks related to them without any issues. Specifying the privacy of
the review also was clear and did not cause any problems.
Viewing the ratings and reviews on the Book Info page also did not pose any problems.
When tasked to check the specific rating parameters, some of the users tapped the overall
rating which automatically scrolled them down to the correct section, some scrolled there
manually. Both groups, however, were able to find the required piece of information.
Viewing the reviews and changing their sorting order was very clear - all of the users
immediately knew what to do. Also, marking the book as ”finished” and navigating to the
Rate This Book page itself was performed by everyone effortlessly.
6.4.3
Other comments
In general, users had a good impression of our prototype. They found it easy to navigate
and said that interaction feels better than with Goodreads. One of them also liked that
there were various ways to personalize the experience in the app and various reminders or
tips regarding all the additional options. Another user also appreciated this, yet suggested
that there can be even more provided.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
87
==PAGE:94==
6.4.4
Survey results
As mentioned before, at the end of testing every participant filled in NASA TLX, SUS, and
our custom survey.
As we skipped the pairwise comparison step in NASA TLX, the scores presented in Ta-
ble 6.1 are raw averages without any weighting. Nevertheless, it is clear that all of the
users performed the tasks without much effort, as the total average of all the user scores
is 9.5 on a scale from 0 to 100 with 19.17 being the highest score from user P2 and 2.5
being the lowest score from user P3. When it comes to individual metrics, Frustration has
the lowest average value of 2, with only two users marking it above 0. Both Performance
and Mental have relatively high values, 18 and 15, respectively.

6.5
Analysis using 10 Usability Heuristics for UI Design
6.5.1
Visibility of system status
There are multiple ways in which the system provides appropriate and timely feedback,
such as with notifications (e.g., adding a book to ”favorites”), the book status banners
overlaid on the cover in the Book Info page, and the clear information about shelf and
review privacy settings. There are, however, also elements that remain unclear, such as
the book format icon on the Book Info page, which was custom-made. This could be made
better by merely testing different kinds of icons to see which is the most clear to users. We
also found through user testing that for a couple of users, it was unclear that the overall
rating on the Rate This Book page was meant to be manually indicated by the user rather
than being automatically calculated based on the user’s other rating parameter inputs.
One way this issue could be addressed is by hiding the secondary rating parameters
until after the user inputs his/her overall rating. These interactive ratings on the Rate
This Book page could also be made more clear by displaying the numerical value of the
inputted rating next to the stars.
6.5.2
Match between system and the real world
Using language that is familiar to our users was an interesting challenge for us in some
respects because we have various user types. Beyond the five archetypes we identified,
we also attempted to design for users who are familiar with book-tracking, as well as
for those who are not. As examined in the previous chapter, this particular difference in
background accounts for a different foundational understanding of book-tracking language
and concepts. For example, while the word ”read” is commonly used in the past tense
as the name of the shelf or collection of books users have read, we found that for newer
users, the word was misleading. Some interpreted it in the present tense, which could
indicate something similar to ”reading” or even as a note-to-self of books the user wants
to read. We decided to confront this by replacing the term with ”finished”, which we found
was more clear for all users. On the other hand of the spectrum, we also incorporated a
default shelf called ”Did not finish”, which is meant to refer to the books the user started
but discontinued. This phrase is well-known in most book-tracking communities and was
requested a number of times in the Goodreads app reviews. During testing, we quickly
found that users who were not familiar with book-tracking had to ask what it meant. While
we considered swapping it for a clearer term, we ultimately decided that the phrasing was
too distinct and it had become too customary within book-tracking to easily replace in this
context.
Additionally, we experimented with the terms ”Add to shelf” and ”Status”, for saving a
book from the Book Info page, which each resonated more with different users. For new
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
89
==PAGE:96==
book-tracking users particularly, it seemed that the first term was intuitive when adding the
book to their ”Want to read” shelf. However, when marking the book as ”finished”, which
is essentially the same as moving it to their ”Finished” shelf, they were more inclined to
understand the term ”Status”. While our fifth and final prototype incorporates the latter
term, we would ideally like to further iterate this element to better refine it.
Many of the icons used are standard graphics meant to convey typical functionalities (e.g.,
the heart icon is used to favorite a book, the pen and notepad icon is used to write a quick-
note, etc.). Certain icons can still be improved, however, such as the book format icon
mentioned above.
6.5.3
User control and freedom
The system includes various opportunities to cancel or exit unwanted or mistaken actions,
such as:
• the ”Undo” button, which appears after removing a book from the Shelf page,
• the ”Cancel” option on various pop-ups and modals (e.g., when adding a new shelf
or when writing a quick-note),
• the ”Back” button to navigate to a previous page.
While some of these features are consistent across the system, such as the ”Back” button,
others are not (e.g., there is no clearly labeled ”cancel” option on the ”Congratulations”
feedback modal). There should also be more ”Undo” buttons (or something equivalent),
such as when a user accidentally dismisses a book recommendation within a shelf or
marks a book as ”finished” from the Library page.
6.5.4
Consistency and standards
In order to maintain consistency and standards to better eliminate the need for users to
guess functionalities, we tried to follow various industry practices and common solutions
by incorporating the following:
• sticking to one accent color for all interactive elements,
• using a swiping gesture to remove a book from a shelf,
• using common icons and control (e.g., buttons),
• sticking to a similar UI layout of book-tracking apps,
• maintaining a fixed color scheme and font styles throughout,
• using the same terms everywhere (e.g., the ”Finished” shelf, the status dropdown
menu, etc.).
We believe we could improve by fixing the term ”Status” to something more relevant be-
cause it led to issues during testing. Few users recognized that it was how to place a book
in their ”Want to read” shelf. It then worked better when asking people to ”mark the book
as ’finished’”, but we suspect this might also be because they had familiarized themselves
with the term by then. We were also somewhat inconsistent with the info icons in some
places (e.g., in the shelves).
6.5.5
Error prevention
The system does not request confirmation after the user indicates they want to remove
a book from a shelf. Instead, users are presented with an ”Undo” button, as described
earlier in Section 6.5.3. Our intention in doing this was to minimize the number of clicks
(i.e., taps) required for the action, but this could be unnecessary if it’s an infrequent action.
90
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:97==
There is also no way to prevent accidentally publishing a review/rating of a book if the ”Fin-
ish” button is tapped. However, the published reviews/ratings are editable, as indicated
by the pen icon. One way we can better prevent premature publishing is by presenting
users with a brief preview of their ratings and review after indicating they are finished,
which can then serve as confirmation that the user wants to proceed or go back.
Another unresolved issue would arise if the user accidentally changes the status of a book
to ”finished”. It’s unclear still how the system would handle ”re-reads” or books that users
have read multiple times. If the user designates a book as ”Finished” and then changes it
to ”Want to read”, should the book remain in the ”Finished” shelf? We have yet to develop
a clear approach for this matter.
One way in which we do implement a form of error prevention in the system is through our
use of inactive buttons in certain pop-ups (e.g., writing a quick-note). In these cases, the
continue buttons remain inactive until the interactive fields are sufficiently filled in, which
prevents the user from advancing before properly completing the action.
6.5.6
Recognition rather than recall
To minimize the need for users to recall information, we have implemented various mea-
sures of providing help in context (e.g., info icons and explanatory text). We also received
a comment from one of the users during our final testing that while the system contains
many unique features, it also does a good job of reminding the users of what they can do
wherever relevant.
Nonetheless, we have identified multiple instances in which the users have to remember
the meaning or functionality of an element, such as with several icons that include no
explanation. The phrase ”Did not finish” is also only explained in the Library page but
nowhere else, even though it is a default shelf option and is therefore always present in
the status dropdown menu.
6.5.7
Flexibility and efficiency of use
To promote flexibility and ways to tailor the user experience of the system for both new and
expert users, we have implemented various shortcuts. For example, to remove a book
from a shelf, a new user can do this by going into the Book Info page and either changing
the status to ”None” or editing the custom shelf menu, depending on which shelf the user
wants to remove the book from. A quicker way to complete this action is by going directly
to the shelf and swiping left on the book entry. If not for the limitations we encountered
with using Figma, we would have also liked to incorporate swiping left on the screens in
order to navigate to a previous page (i.e., a ”back” shortcut), as is customary in many
mobile applications.
Another example involved marking a book as ”finished”, which can either be done through
the Book Info page using the status dropdown menu or directly from the Library page
using the shortcut button labeled ”Mark as finished”. While this shortcut is not necessarily
”hidden” from new users, which is often characteristic of typical shortcuts, it still allows for
flexibility in the user experience, depending on what feels most intuitive for users.
The Book Info page also includes a shortcut that originated from feedback during some
of our user tests, which suggests that it might be helpful for both new and expert users
alike. This shortcut involves tapping on the book’s overall (primary) rating at the top of
the page to be navigated directly to the secondary rating parameters, which are otherwise
accessed by scrolling down on the page.
We noticed during some thinking-aloud tests that users were attempting to long-press the
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
91
==PAGE:98==
book entries to complete certain actions. Interestingly, we had intentionally avoided im-
plementing any long-press actions because in the first user testing, we received feedback
saying that users would not think to do this. While further testing is required, this could be
an example in which users sometimes don’t know what they really want.
6.5.8
Aesthetic and minimalist design
We did our best to maintain a simple design throughout the system by including only rel-
evant and important information and elements. Regardless, we are also aware that we’re
not experienced UI designers and because of this, there is definite room for improvement.
For example, we received feedback during our final user testing that the book recommen-
dation feature in the ”Want to read” shelf resembles an advertisement, which may cause
users to subconsciously disregard it. This could be a result, in part, of using a generally
subdued color scheme, which was a challenge for us to develop throughout the prototyp-
ing phase. We tried to find a balance between being visually modern and interesting but
not overly childish. We also used two main font faces across the pages, which helped to
convey hierarchy; one was used exclusively for page headers and book titles while the
other was used for all other text (except, by mistake, in the shared shelf collaborators pop-
up). We also tried to implement a visual hierarchy through the use of shadows, colors,
and the sizing of elements (e.g., book covers). Additionally, by minimizing the number
of sharp corners (i.e., using rounded corners) throughout, we hoped to strengthen the
modern appeal of the system. Ultimately, we feel that the UI does feel minimal, but lacks
visual interest and could be improved by experimenting more with color themes and other
visual elements.
6.5.9
Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors
As our prototype is still in a relatively early stage, we have not yet considered errors and
how users would interact with them.
6.5.10
Help and documentation
As described in Section 6.5.6, we have attempted to incorporate help in context, by using
the info icons or plan informational text. We did not, however, develop a ”Help Center”,
as we considered that out of scope for this project, which is focusing on aspects of an
app rather than the full app. For the same reason, there is no tutorial page. However, it
might be beneficial to have more in-context and brief tutorials, which could appear during
the user’s first time using the different parts of the app. We believe this would amount to
a simpler user experience than if the system presented an overall, longer app tutorial in
the ”set-up” phase of the app, which would then require users to recall specific notes of
information and have to re-read or re-watch the tutorial if necessary.
