7
Discussion
7.1
Our solution via the lens of the problem statement
Based on the results from the final validation, we believe that our solution for evaluat-
ing books was ultimately stronger than our solution for managing collections. First and
foremost, managing collections is a more comprehensive activity that involves a broad
number of tasks, some of which we failed to implement (e.g., deleting shelves, editing
shelf names, etc.). Comparatively, evaluating books is more straightforward, allowing us
to spend more time developing fewer features.
Our defined problem statement based on the user research was: how might we make
managing collections and evaluating books more intuitive, flexible, findable, and easy to
navigate for both new and existing book-tracking users between the ages of 18 and 35?
In the following subsections, we evaluate how the final prototype addresses these four
specified objectives to improve the user experience of book-tracking overall.
7.1.1
Intuitive
For the system to be successfully intuitive, it should be usable without needing instruction
or direction.
Managing collections
The results from the thinking-aloud tests using the itemized task list suggest that the man-
aging collections aspect of the system was only partially successful in this respect. Some
tasks were more intuitive to complete than others. For example, the quick-add function
(i.e., quickly adding a book to one’s ”Want to read” shelf directly from either the Discover
page or a shared shelf) caused no issues. However, the slow-add process (i.e., adding a
book to one’s ”Want to read” shelf from the Book Info page) was not immediately intuitive
to most users. They did not easily make the connection between the task ”add the book
to your ’Want to read’ shelf” and the status dropdown.
When examining the issue using the 10 Usability Heuristics, we are able to further isolate
and understand this problem. We suspect the word ”status” didn’t register with users for
two main reasons; the first is that the word deviates from typical book-tracking terminol-
ogy, which usually sounds more similar to e.g., ”add to shelf” or ”save book”. This irreg-
ularity reflects a potential issue with heuristic #2, Match between system & real world,
because it may be that users are generally more inclined to seek buttons that use the
above-mentioned book-tracking language. In regards to the users who were not pre-
viously familiar with book-tracking, we believe the wording of the given task, which also
used typical book-tracking terms, misled them during the thinking-aloud. This also closely
relates to heuristic #4, Consistency and standards, because no other part of the system
uses the word ”status”, but in multiple areas the word ”shelf” is used. Moreover, we used
the concept of a library containing various shelves as the base structure for organizing
books in the system.
To resolve the uncertainty around the slow-add process, one possible strategy would be to
revert the word ”status” back to ”add to shelf”, which we had previously implemented. The
reason we had initially changed from the ”Add to shelf” button to the status dropdown in the
fourth iteration was that users who were unfamiliar with book-tracking did not understand
that the button would also be used for marking the book as ”finished”. Unlike the issue
that arose in the final prototype testing, users found it more intuitive when adding the book
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
99
==PAGE:106==
to their ”Want to read” shelf, but not for the subsequent task, which was ”mark the book as
’read’”. In retrospect, this problem was likely also a result of the task wording as well as
the fact that the button maintained the label ”add to shelf” even after the user had added
the book to their ”Want to read” shelf.
Ultimately, the slow-add button or dropdown should be labeled with a term that is familiar to
users, consistent with the system, and ideally clear to newcomers. We speculate that the
original ”Add to shelf” button could be a better fit if the words were automatically revised
to, e.g., ”Change shelf” after users first add the book to a shelf. We also believe that in
some cases, new users merely need to learn certain aspects of a system. In the context of
our solution, it’s probable that users would learn the slow-add process in a short amount
of time.
We found that the rest of the managing collections portion of the system had a moderately
clear UI. Aside from the slow-add process, the remaining tasks in the thinking-aloud test
posed few to no issues among users. Nevertheless, in a future iteration, we would want
to modify the in-shelf book recommendation card to appear less like an advertisement.
We would also like to adjust the placement of the filter icon in the Shelf view so that it is
closer to the sorting dropdown.
In relation to the Usability Heuristics, managing collections performs well on the following
fronts:
• Visibility of system status,
• Consistency and standards (not including the slow-add process),
• Recognition rather than recall,
• Flexibility and efficiency of use,
• Help & documentation.
Conversely, improvements could be focused in the following areas:
• User control and freedom - with more consistent cancel and undo buttons,
• Error prevention - with a plan to prevent accidentally marking a book as ”finished”,
• Aesthetic and minimalist design - with an overall design update.
Evaluating books
It was clear to us that the book evaluation page was more straightforward based on the
ease with which users completed the associated tasks during the thinking-aloud tests.
They were able to easily assign both full-star and half-star ratings and understood how to
use the rating parameters menu without requiring further direction or guidance. Operating
the review prompts feature also appeared to be intuitive.
In general, the process of evaluating books adequately adheres to most of the Usability
Heuristics. The most room for improvement lies within heuristic #5, Error prevention,
and heuristic #7, Flexibility and efficiency of use. In regards to the former, there could be
some confirmation prompt prior to users publishing a review in order to prevent them from
accidentally doing so. To promote the latter, we could implement some shortcut methods
for rating a book quickly, such as by allowing users to assign the book’s overall rating
immediately after marking it as ”finished” (on the Book Info page).
100
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:107==
As a whole, the book evaluation process is likely a familiar process to new and experi-
enced book-tracking users alike because it resembles typical rating and reviewing sys-
tems from other platforms (e.g., shopping websites).
NASA TLX and SUS surveys
Overall, the NASA TLX and SUS survey results were generally positive, which may give
some indication of the intuitiveness of the system overall. However, although they pro-
vided us with quantitative data to compound the qualitative feedback we obtained, the
surveys were not as useful as we hoped they would be. The main issue comes from the
lack of data we could use to compare them to and the very limited number of responses.
When distributing the NASA TLX survey, we did not ask users to perform pairwise com-
parisons, as we understood them to be optional. In the end, we learned that it is also an
important step in conducting this survey, but we were not able to collect the missing data.
This makes the results of NASA TLX less accurate and relevant than they could be, yet we
still think they are helpful as they show us the general impression of the workload coming
from using our prototype. When it comes to the SUS survey, the fidelity of our prototype
might be considered too low to be properly assessed by this tool. Our implementation is
only part of the potential whole book-tracking application. Although we tried making the
testing experience as smooth as possible, there were plenty of elements that were not in-
teractive in any way. It significantly simplifies the whole system, hiding potential usability
problems in case of the full solution, and may cause the scores to be artificially high.
7.1.2
Flexible
For the system to be successfully flexible, it should adapt to unique user preferences and
provide users sufficient freedom to express their views. This objective also closely relates
to the general usefulness of the system as described in Morville’s UX Honeycomb [35].
Managing collections
Within the library and shelves, we believe the following features contribute to the overall
flexibility of our solution:
• ability to add custom shelves,
• shared shelf,
• search, sort, and filter capabilities,
• toggle between list and grid view of a shelf,
• ”Currently reading” card in the Library view,
• quick-notes to provide context to books in shelves,
• privacy settings for each individual shelf.
Notably, the shared shelf concept provides an added layer of flexibility to managing collec-
tions that we had not seen in any book-tracking platform during our research. By allowing
users to collaborate within shelves, the system presents users with a unique opportunity
to stay connected with friends and their latest reads. We also felt the shared shelf would
be an interesting approach to bridging the gap we observed within our user research.
While users highly value book recommendations from friends, they rarely use the exist-
ing book-recommending features within the current platforms. The shared shelf poses
a unique, more connected way in which users can recommend books, and users were
excited by the idea during testing. Even still, further testing with a higher fidelity prototype
is required to evaluate how successful this idea actually is.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
101
==PAGE:108==
Unlike the shared shelf, which was a new idea, the ”Currently reading” card in the Library
view is something we observed in existing platforms (e.g., Goodreads). We intentionally
incorporated this element in our solution because we felt that it works well at providing
users flexibility in how they interact with the books they’re currently reading. Depending
on their preferences, users can quickly mark a book as ”finished” directly from the Library
page, or they can enter the Book Info page to modify the book’s status there. Additionally,
multiple books can be displayed simply through horizontal scrolling.
The quick-notes feature was an idea that sparked during user research in response to
a need expressed in the interviews and app reviews. Users wanted to be able to add
context to certain books when adding them to a shelf, so as to remember why they saved
the book in the first place. We believe that when taking the user journey from Section 4.3.3
into account, implementing the ability to write quick-notes for saved books will benefit the
Decide phase. This essentially means that in the circumstances when users are browsing
their shelves to find the next book to read, having contextual notes will hopefully simplify
the process and decrease the amount of work users need to do to make said decision.
During testing, we received positive feedback from all users regarding this feature, which
further validates its usefulness. We also believe that there may be circumstances in which
users don’t want to add context to a book. In this case, the quick-notes feature is versatile
enough to provide users the freedom to decide what information gets attached to the
book. For example, in one of the thinking-aloud tests, a user expressed that the quick-
notes feature could also be useful for actually documenting important notes while reading.
This is why we have also ensured that users can access their quick-notes while evaluating
the books. We wanted to be sure to provide sufficient flexibility for users to decide how
the feature would be most helpful to them.
Lastly, more specific privacy control was another common need expressed during user
research. We, therefore, implemented individualized privacy settings in various parts of
the system. Within managing collections, users are able to dictate the privacy of each of
their shelves, including the default ones. This allows them the power to decide how visible
their saved books are and to whom, which we believe makes the system more flexible
than existing solutions (e.g., one overall privacy setting determines whether users’ entire
profiles are public or private in Goodreads).
Evaluating books
Within the Rate This Book page, we believe the following features contribute to the overall
flexibility of our solution:
• secondary rating parameters,
• review prompts for assistance,
• privacy settings for each review,
• quick-notes access during evaluation,
• ability to save drafts and edit reviews.
Two of the most popular features of our entire system include the descriptive rating param-
eters and the review prompts. Users were often most excited about these components
because of the way in which they help to specify, articulate, and structure users’ thoughts
on a book.
The rating parameters idea is similar to what we had observed in another platform, Sto-
ryGraph. There, the parameters are predefined and inflexible, although each is optional
to complete. While we see this being more useful than Goodreads’ single rating and
102
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:109==
open text field, we also feel that the list of questions can be daunting to some, and poten-
tially discourage users from evaluating the books. This is why in our solution, the rating
parameters are chosen by the user, depending on which aspects of the book they find
most relevant to rate. The layout of the ratings is also intentionally concise, so as to not
overwhelm users.
The review prompts idea was inspired by online dating apps, e.g., Hinge. Users of these
platforms are provided with question prompts when constructing a profile to help them
think about the most relevant or interesting details about themselves. In the context of
book-tracking, we felt that the review prompts feature would make a useful tool for users
who sometimes struggle to articulate or structure their thoughts about a book after read-
ing. We designed this feature to be included as extra assistance and not as a default
component of the evaluation page so that users are not overwhelmed unnecessarily.
7.1.3
Findable
For the system to be successfully findable, it should present an easy way of locating
essential features and functions.
In general, to make it easy for the users to locate all the interactive and most important
features we tried to tie semantic meaning to colors. The vast majority of the elements
which are clickable have a specific shade of blue. Also, normal text (e.g., book descrip-
tions or reviews) is black, while secondary, slightly less important text (e.g., book series
name, review posting date) is grey. Although it is not written explicitly anywhere, users
quickly notice those semantics and expect interactivity from all the elements matching the
pattern. This helps us to highlight the most essential elements.
Another thing globally helping with locating important elements is the usage of shadows
and size hierarchy. In the Library, for example, book sizes are smaller, as we want to
draw attention to the individual shelves. On the other hand, in the Shelf view, the book
sizes are larger as they are more important for this page. The biggest book covers are
visible on the Book Info page, as that is the crucial part of book representation. Similarly,
we have two standard button types - bigger, solid blue buttons (”hard buttons”) which
allow the users to perform the most important actions in the given context, and smaller,
blue buttons without background (”soft buttons”) to provide access to secondary actions.
Also, to spotlight unique and important elements of the pages we added shadows to their
frames in order to add depth and make them appear closer to the user, e.g., the book
recommendation on the Shelf view.
Managing collections
An essential feature of the managing collections area is the ability to search, sort, and
filter the books on a shelf. In our prototype, users can find the books they are interested
in by their title, author, genres, and other criteria. It allows them to narrow down their
search rather than scroll through a long list of books. When it comes to the placement of
the three functionalities, although they are present, they are located in two separate parts
of the screen. We learned in the last iteration that they should be in close proximity, as is
typical in other platforms.
Another thing we learned through user testing is that users value positive feedback when
finishing a book. In Goodreads, users need to intentionally navigate to their profile to view
their updated reading challenge progress. In our solution, the users are automatically
presented with a modal showing all their progress and achievement information upon
finishing a book. This immediate feedback attaches a greater sense of achievement to
the action of marking the book as ”finished”.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
103
==PAGE:110==
Evaluating books
On the Rate This Book page, maintaining an interface that is both concise and simple
was important to us to prevent overwhelming users. The page consists of four basic sec-
tions divided by subtle horizontal lines. The sections include the overall rating, secondary
ratings, review text box, and privacy settings, all of which include only the most essen-
tial pieces of information for completing the form. Additional features, which can aid the
review process (e.g., review prompts), are accessible via the blue ”soft buttons”.
On the Book Info page users can access the secondary rating information by tapping
the overall, primary rating at the top of the page which automatically scrolls the view
down to the secondary parameters section. This was implemented based on behaviors
we observed during testing in which users were intuitively performing this action with the
expectation that it would reveal the secondary parameters.
7.1.4
Easy to navigate
For the system to be successfully easy to navigate, it should be easy for users to move
around.
While we initially envisioned more distinction between the terms ”easy to navigate” and
”findable”, we have come to the conclusion they are closely related, and ”easy to navigate”
involves elements that are findable and help the users move around the app. Therefore,
the ways in which we view the system to be easy to navigate overlap with the way in which
it is findable.
To make sure that the prototype is easy to navigate, we focused on making interactive ele-
ments easy to click - the buttons are sized according to the industry standards1, collapsible
fields are interactive all over their area instead of single spot where the arrow/”Read more”
text is located, and a Shelf view can be opened by tapping any part of its card in the Li-
brary view. On the other hand, we were not fully able to test and get realistic feedback
on the ease of interaction - very often Figma was not correctly registering taps, especially
on smaller elements, preventing the users from moving around smoothly. For example,
this issue occurred often when tapping the ”Back” arrow, which is a frequently performed
action.
Also, to keep the navigation consistent, we kept the general layout of the pages as similar
as possible - all of them include a header consisting of a ”Back” button, optional title, and/or
icons, then the main content of the page which is vertically scrollable and tab navigation
at the bottom. We left the last piece undeveloped, as it was outside our scope, yet it was
still important to include it in the prototype as a placeholder. The other functionality we
are missing from the prototype is swiping to go back. We think it would be a valuable
addition to the smooth navigation experience, but it was not easily achievable in Figma,
so we decided to leave it out.
7.1.5
Custom survey
While our custom survey showed us user opinions directly related to the problem state-
ment, it was not as informative as we had hoped. The main reason is the lack of any
baselines or standard ways of interpreting the scores. Even though the average scores
for all the statements were around 90, it is hard to put those numbers into perspective or
compare them with other ways of evaluation. Maybe it would be beneficial if we asked
the test participants to fill out the surveys for each of our focus areas (i.e., managing col-
lections, and evaluating books). This way we would be able to better pinpoint the origin
of the issues.
1https://uxmovement.com/mobile/optimal-size-and-spacing-for-mobile-buttons/ (accessed: 2023-06-17)
104
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:111==
The best use of the data from our custom survey is to compare individual scales with
each other. Doing this suggests that users believe the aspect we succeeded in the most
is ”flexible”, meaning the users felt satisfied with the freedom to express their views and
preferences.
7.2
Our high-level goal and the book-tracking market
As stated in the Introduction of this report, our high-level goal was to promote reading and
its inherent benefits. Based on our research, we believe that book-tracking is a valuable
tool for enhancing the reading experience. With our solution - although confronting only
a segment of the user journey - we contribute to an improved user experience of book-
tracking, and therefore reading in general.
As we inferred early on, Goodreads holds the throne of dedicated book-tracking apps
which support all book formats (i.e., print, digital, audio). Platforms like Amazon Kindle
and Libby have also paved their own paths in the market by supporting in-app reading
and listening. Questions still remain, however, regarding what kind of similar app will be
able to compete with Goodreads in the future. From our point of view, StoryGraph, while
young and filled with potential, presents a risk of losing users early on by promising too
much and executing important features inadequately. We believe that for an app to fully
challenge the leading platform, it needs to do the basic functions Goodreads does, and
it needs to do them well. On top of this, the app would need to tap into one or more
of the stages of the user journey in which Goodreads is weak or nonexistent. We see
opportunities in the following stages:
• Decide - users still struggle when making a choice of which book from their shelves
to pick up next,
• Read - Goodreads has no presence here,
• Judge - the book evaluation is arguably oversimplified,
• Recommend - there exists a notable gap between users wanting book recommen-
dations from friends and using the recommending feature in the app,
• Reflect - the Goodreads reading challenge is a popular feature, but the statistics are
limited.
In others, the platform owned by Amazon does a good job and can be used as a source
of inspiration. Competing with Goodreads there might be significantly harder, especially
taking into account its well-established position on the market.
When it comes to comparing our solution to the existing products, we can say that we
technically achieved all of our implementation goals presented in Table 4.8. However,
depending on their complexity, some features were tested more thoroughly than others.
Regardless, all of them are visually represented in the final Figma prototype. For ex-
ample, we investigated ”context notes for shelved books” in more detail than a ”tagging
system” because it was a new concept that was not yet represented in other book-tracking
platforms. We iterated the former consistently, while the latter was implemented early on
and largely left alone. We did this because we felt that it would require testing with a
high-fidelity prototype (i.e., a real smartphone app) to check its usefulness in the book-
searching process.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
105
==PAGE:112==
7.3
Reflections on the design process
When considering our timeline as a whole, we can safely acknowledge that we managed
to wrap up the final iteration and testing on schedule. This is not to say, however, that
the entire project went as planned. The user research phase, for example, carried on
much longer than we expected it to. We underestimated the time it would take to both
process and analyze the data we obtained and we did not want to sacrifice the quality of
the user research by rushing through it. Ultimately, we feel it is worth spending the time to
truly understand the context of the users and their needs. Because of this and due to the
nature of design thinking, up until the last iteration, we continued revisiting and redefining
our problem statement as we continued to learn about our users.
We then encountered an impasse when revisiting the existing solutions using the insights
we had learned from our research. At this point in the middle of the design process, we
realized that if we were to attempt to design an entire book-tracking app, we would be
over-extending ourselves in the allotted time frame. Doing so would essentially prevent
us from the opportunity to create tangible, impressionable change. Thus, we pivoted and
narrowed our scope. From this point on, we used the defined stages of our user journey
to examine and select the two areas of book-tracking on which to center our efforts.
Afterward, we reached the prototyping phase of the process, which was therefore more
compact than we intended it to be, as we wanted to iterate around five times before finaliz-
ing. This meant in our limited remaining time, we developed each iteration over the course
of about one week, then tested the updated prototypes each weekend. While it would’ve
been useful to have extra time for this stage of the process, we also felt that the quick
turnaround times for the iterations forced us to prioritize our efforts and try ideas rather
than potentially pondering them for more time. We also noted that we spent a substan-
tial amount of developing aspects of the book-tracking system that were not necessarily
part of our scope, e.g., the overall layout of the Book Info page. We did this because the
Book Info page was necessary for various actions related to both managing collections
and evaluating books. The whole Book Info page layout, while not immediately relevant
to either managing collections or evaluating books, could have potentially influenced the
user experience of the prototype overall. Therefore, in order to ensure that testing pro-
ceeded smoothly, we had to spend time developing this page so that it achieved a level
of completeness that matched the rest of the system.
Throughout the design process, one thing we had initially set out to do but ultimately
skipped was the workshops. We still hold that the workshops would’ve provided great
value to the design process, however, we had to make an executive decision to omit
them. Scheduling one distinct time to meet with four or more people at once was more
challenging than we initially thought it to be. Eventually, we felt that the time required to
organize the workshops would’ve been better spent in other ways and that we could not
afford to further delay our timeline.
7.4
Reflection on methods and tools
7.4.1
Prototyping and testing
Beyond the limitations of the surveys we administered during our final evaluation, we also
want to highlight the weaknesses of the other testing methods we used (i.e., thinking-aloud
and AB testing). With the thinking-aloud tests, users were asked to perform a series of
tasks and verbalize their passing thoughts while doing so. We noticed, however, that
some users were not voicing their unfiltered opinions as much, preventing us from fully
understanding their experience using the prototype. Additionally, we suspect that the
106
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:113==
wording of the tasks strongly biased users in some cases, especially users who were new
to book-tracking. For example, the task ”go to your shared shelf” was meant to prompt
users to find which shelf may be the collaborative shelf, based on some visual indicator.
Instead, users looked for a shelf literally named ”Shared Shelf”. In retrospect, because
of this, it was sometimes challenging for us to decipher whether the issues users ran into
were a result of poor design or misleading task wordings (or both). In regards to the AB
tests, if we could redo them, we would try to be more mindful of changing the order in
which the options were shown, for example using Latin Square Design. This may not
have had a major effect on the final results, but we do still feel that the sequence did bias
the users in some respect.
In total, we maintain that testing with more users would’ve been beneficial. A broader
range of perspectives would not only illuminate a more diverse set of advantages and
disadvantages in the design; it would also potentially provide a stronger sense of vali-
dation (or invalidation), which ultimately makes for stronger reasoning for various design
decisions. Using more reliable quantitative methods would also aid in providing concrete
reasoning for or against certain decisions, which could strengthen the design overall.
When developing the different iterations, we also consistently updated the User Story Map
(USM), which helped loosely guide us to implement a number of the outlined tasks. We
believe, however, that if we spent more time refining the USM, we would’ve been able to
prioritize elements of the design better. We also may have incorporated other important
aspects in the design that we excluded on accident (e.g., deleting shelves or editing shelf
names).
7.4.2
User research
Of all the user research methods we used, each provided its own value. For one, the
survey gave us an initial insight into the values and needs of both book-tracking users and
non-users. It also pointed us towards the features of book-tracking which are the most
and least used, suggesting to us potential opportunities for intervention. While distributing
the survey was relatively easy and we received over 100 responses, only 1/3 came from
current book-tracking users. Knowing this now, we would distribute the survey in more
places over a longer period of time to gather more relevant data. It was still useful for us
to hear from non-users, but a more balanced ratio would have been ideal. Also, although
we attempted to structure the survey in a way that was clear for users and beneficial for
us, in retrospect we see that we could have done better by outlining the user journey
beforehand rather than later. This would have helped in various areas, e.g.:
• preparing the survey - questions would better reflect the different activities involved
in book-tracking compared to us coming up with a list of typical features,
• answering the survey - users could be guided through the survey using the concepts
from the user journey to better understand the questions and avoid misinterpreta-
tions,
• analyzing the results - it would be much easier for us to identify areas of opportunity
or pain points.
The five user interviews allowed us to dive deeper into the minds of a few users to gain
a more thorough understanding of their desires and behaviors. At this point, we outlined
a basic user journey to begin mapping out a typical chain of activities for readers who
use book-tracking. Doing this allowed us to better visualize the different stages and what
obstacles or barriers they might include. It also allowed us to better pinpoint the goals of
each stage and how different design elements might impact these goals. After framing the
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
107
==PAGE:114==
reader journey, we used affinity mapping to then categorize the comments from the inter-
views into each associated journey stage. This was useful in assessing the overall trends,
but with only five users who differed greatly, we found it difficult to make final conclusions
based on these trends. Instead, we found ourselves labeling the trends merely by subject.
Thus, while the interviews were useful, we felt the biggest takeaway was the realization
that we were dealing with a diverse set of users, which needed to be recognized more
precisely moving forward. On the other hand, if we were to conduct the interviews again,
we would have also liked to use the user journey as a guide. Similar to the survey, we
believe that walking through the user journey with the participants would not only provide
a wider range of insights but it would also have simplified our analysis later.
The last of our three main user research methods, the analysis of the Goodreads app
reviews allowed us to quickly scan a large amount of data to see common trends of com-
plaints or unmet needs specified for the platform. Compared to the survey and interviews,
this dataset was significantly larger and more representative of book-tracking users. It
gave us insights into the problematic features of Goodreads, allowing us to develop im-
proved versions of them in our prototype. This led to positive feedback from Goodreads
users during testing. These results were also used to further validate or invalidate the
feedback we received from both previous user research methods. To obtain more value
from this analysis method, we would have liked to access both a larger number of reviews
to better represent the user views and reviews of other book-tracking platforms. This
would have improved our competitive analysis as we would have a better understanding
of popular features or pitfalls of apps aside from Goodreads.
While we initially set out to create personas based on our user research, we ended up
finding more value in generating reader archetypes. Rather than spending time develop-
ing specific details of our users, we wanted to identify and highlight the major behavioral
and motivational trends we observed from the research. Because we had noticed a wide
variety of user types from our interviews, it was important for us to acknowledge these
distinct blends of characteristics in a way that was not too detailed or inflexible.
The competitive analysis was another useful tool in that it allowed us to fully take inventory
of and compare each of the notable players we had identified in the book-tracking market.
It also showed us the most common functionalities of book-tracking solutions. We learned,
however, that just because an app or platform supports some function or feature does not
imply that it does so effectively. This was difficult for us to convey in the competitive
analysis tables, although there may be an alternative way of scoring the platforms in each
category rather than simply assigning a binary value, which could help mitigate this issue.
7.5
Future work
The current state of the prototype can give an idea of how collection management and
book evaluation should be implemented. It is, however, by no means a prototype that
could be used instantly as a base for developing a market-ready mobile application for
book-tracking.
Even taking into account only its two focus areas, they should be developed and tested
further. The prototype should include important functionalities which are currently missing,
e.g., deleting or renaming shelves. Then, a higher fidelity prototype should be developed.
The development process itself is likely to reveal other issues which should be addressed
at this point.
Before full implementation, there should also be much more user testing. By the end
of our last iteration, we managed to perform only 15 tests. While at this early stage, it
108
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:115==
was enough to get plenty of comments for improvement, the tests were not necessarily
exhaustive - we did not reach the point where the majority of the feedback repeats. Ac-
cording to Nielsen and Landauer [44], five testers should be enough to reveal many of the
usability problems and that should be the target number of tests for every iteration.
It might be also beneficial to research popular features and pitfalls of the other apps on
the market. While we performed a competitive analysis and discovered what these apps
can do, we do not know user opinions about how well are they performing. App reviews
analysis turned out to be very valuable in discovering problems of that type and it should
be performed also for the other existing book-tracking apps. It can help in understanding
what users want much better as well as avoid mistakes in implementing potentially useful
features.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
109
==PAGE:116==
110
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:117==
8
Conclusion
We initially set out to improve the user experience of book-tracking in order to contingently
further promote reading for pleasure. At the outset of this project, we aimed to answer
the general question:
How might we improve the user experience of book-tracking mobile apps?
Through 104 survey responses, five user interviews, and nearly 1000 app reviews, we
learned that the experience of managing collections is often overly complex, limited, and
unclear. Additionally, the process of evaluating books is either considered rigid and inflex-
ible or difficult to navigate and confusing. Taking these findings into account, we asked
ourselves:
How might we make managing collections and evaluating books more intuitive,
flexible, findable, and easy to navigate for both new and existing book-tracking
users between the ages of 18 and 35?
Through an iterative design process, which involved a total of 15 user tests across five
iterations, we confronted this question by ultimately creating a prototype that is usable
without needing instruction, adapts to unique user preferences, and provides the freedom
to sufficiently express user views. We also ensured the solution enables an easy way of
locating essential features and functions and of moving around in general.
This solution was ultimately evaluated by five users through thinking-aloud tests, the
NASA TLX and SUS surveys, and our own custom survey. The users were both pleased
and enthusiastic about the system overall, often inquiring about whether we planned to
fully develop and publish the app in the future. An expert evaluation was also conducted
utilizing usability heuristics, in which the system effectively fulfills the objectives, not with-
out limitations.
Although the prototype is not a full application, it provides a compelling example of how we
might foster a better user experience in book-tracking. While our solution would benefit
from further development, we have already seen promising results that indicate the impact
it can have on book-tracking as a whole.
In addition to our mid-fidelity prototype, our project outputs include an outline of system
micro-interactions, user validation notes, defined reader archetypes, an adapted user
journey, iterated user story maps, and a competitive analysis of the book-tracking market.

User Research - Interviews - Interview
Guide
132
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:139==
Reminders 
- 
Ask to record the interview if possible 
- 
Try to avoid yes or no questions 
- 
Go with the flow. Follow up their responses with more questions. Always ask WHY. 
- 
Try to not lead them into saying something specific. Keep the questions open-ended.  
 
Questions 
- 
How would you describe yourself as a reader? 
o Do you read frequently? Are you consistent? 
o When do you usually read? For how long? 
o What genre of books are you most drawn to? 
o What format(s) do you read? Print, digital, audio, other? Why? 
▪ 
If more than one, how would you describe the ratio between the time you spend with each? 
o How do you feel about the amount of time you spend reading?  
 
- 
If you read digitally, what app do you use? Why? 
o How would you describe the main features of this app?  
o How do you feel about this app as a whole? Why? 
o What stands out to you about what works well and what doesn’t work well? 
 
- 
Do you keep track of the books you’ve read? If so, how? If not, why not? 
o How do you remember how you felt about those books? 
 
- 
How do you find new books you want to read? Walk me through your process? 
o If you use an app, how do you feel about the recommendations it gives you? 
 
App features we can ask about 
- 
Customizable display preferences for e-book 
- 
Ability to organize personal collections of books 
- 
Social community aspect (friends) 
- 
Social community aspect (chat forums or online book clubs) 
- 
Star rating ability  (half-star ratings?) 
- 
Book progress information (or DNF function) 
- 
Reviews  
o Privacy (public or private) 
o Prompts or no prompts 
- 
Annotation abilities 
- 
Recommendation algorithm  
- 
Personal statistics (tracking your reading) 
- 
Setting reading goals 
- 
Setting reading reminders 
- 
Integration with other reading apps 
- 
Badges/achievement aspect with progress 
- 
Discovering new books 
- 
TBR list 
- 
Up-next queue  
- 
Ability to add books that you already own in print to collections 
==PAGE:140==
C
User Research - Interviews - Interview
summaries
C.0.1
Results
This section will introduce each of the five users we interviewed (using pseudo names)
and summarize the insights gathered from each. To read more detailed notes from each
interview, please refer to Appendix D.
I1 - Samantha
The first user we interviewed was Samantha, a 25-year-old female student at DTU who
generally reads 3-5 books per month, often wavering between science fiction and self-help
or philosophical books. She shared with us that she has been using Goodreads for 4-5
years and originally downloaded it to keep up with what her friends are reading. Now, she
uses Goodreads primarily to keep track of the books she wants to read and overall, she
rates it five out of five stars. We asked her about why she thinks Goodreads is so popular
and she said, ”It has so many users, which makes the reviews feel more trustworthy.”
Interestingly, she shared with us that she usually waits to read reviews until after reading
a book so that she can prevent being spoiled or biased. She also said that some online
reviews can be purchased, which is important to look out for as a user. Regardless, if a
person she knows leaves a review, she knows she can trust it.
During our first card-sorting activity, we asked Samantha to categorize a variety of com-
mon book-tracking features based on how frequently she uses them, with as many tiers as
she feels are needed. Her resulting groups are seen in Figure C.1. We also encouraged
her to add a feature to the list if she feels anything is missing. Accordingly, she chose
to add two features, which were ”reading reviews of other people” and ”finding books
through author”; both of which were included in her top tier or ”most used features” group,
including the following others:
• ”rating/reviewing books you’ve read”,
• ”seeing what your friends are reading”,
• ”discovering new books to read”,
• ”organizing the books you’ve read and want to read into collections”.
Her least used feature was ”participating in reading challenges”, which she explained by
saying she has ”this philosophy that reading should not be a competitive sport.” She went
on to say ”the whole point of reading is to get new viewpoints; get new ideas about your
life. So it’s not about the quantity, it’s about the quality.”
We then asked Samantha how she would rank the features based on which she enjoys
using the most, as opposed to the previous prompt about which she objectively uses the
most. Her ranking leaned toward categorizing based on ease of use, which is captured in
Figure C.2. It shows ”reading reviews of other people” at the top (and therefore easiest to
do) and ”organizing the books you’ve read and want to read into collections” at the bottom.
On ”discovering new books to read”, she described that many apps and websites provide
recommendations merely based on what books users have previously read, without taking
into account what exactly they enjoyed or didn’t enjoy, or what they want. ”Usually the
algorithm doesn’t understand this,” she said, ”they’re just like suggesting based on what
you read and sometimes it just doesn’t make sense.”
134
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:141==
Figure C.1: I1 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) used features of book-tracking
Our next card sorting activity involved categorizing a collection of personal statistics based
on the user’s interest level. Samantha’s resulting grouping is shown in Figure C.3, which
places ”most common qualities of the authors whose work you read”, ” how many different
genres you’ve read (and which)”, and ”most common qualitative trends among the books
you’ve read” as the most interesting data. She also later brought up an idea for a new
statistic, which was ”your rating vs other people’s ratings”, which she ultimately ranked in
the middle tier according to her personal interest level in it.
The final card sorting activity focused on influential factors during book selection. For
Samantha, she described her categories, which can be seen in Figure C.4, from left to
right by saying, ”these are the most that I depend on when I’m trying to find a new book.
These [...] I take [...] into consideration, but not that much. These are the ones that I really
don’t care that much about.” She was especially enthusiastic about ”author” as a factor
because, ”usually when you read one book from an author... and you like the writing style,
then you know the next book is good.” She also mentioned that the subject of a book is
important to her, but not as much as writing style, which often determines if a book is great
in her mind.
Due to having extra time, we then incorporated a short design activity with Samantha in
which we co-created ways to improve some of her least enjoyed or least used features of
book-tracking. The ideas we generated included:
• reading challenges that are more creative or qualitative (not just based on numbers,
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
135
==PAGE:142==
Figure C.2: I1 card sorting results: most enjoyed features of book-tracking
but also type of book, etc.)
• keywords and tags incorporated not only for book collections but also for review
filtering
• more privacy settings (not everyone wants their reviews to be public)
We then wrapped up the interview with Samantha (as with all of the others) by thanking
her for her time and for sharing her insights and experiences with us.
I2 - Emma
Our second interview was with 26 year old Emma, another DTU student who claims she is
always reading at least one book, but doesn’t necessarily go through them quickly. In total,
she says she reads about 12-13 books annually and may sometimes switch between read
the same book in print and in audio format, which she prefers when she is doing activities
around the house like cleaning or cooking. Her genre of choice is usually fiction, at one
point also mentioning a love for Greek mythology.
To Emma, book-tracking gives her an inner sense of achievement. ”It’s really satisfying
to have it recorded, even if I don’t always write reviews,” she shared with us, ”There’s
something really satisfying about [completing] a checklist.” She likes browsing the books
136
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:143==
Figure C.3: I1 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) interesting personal statistics
she’s read on Goodreads, as well as looking for new books to read, which is partly why
she’s been using it since around 2012. Additionally, she told us she doesn’t know of any
other book-tracking apps besides Goodreads.
In terms of the core social aspect of Goodreads, Emma explained that she doesn’t really
care for those features such as scrolling through her newsfeed, seeing what her friends
are reading, joining the online discussion boards or groups, etc. What’s more is that
some of her friends use the platform as well, but not frequently so she sometimes gets
worried about spamming them and can feel embarrassed. Considering this, she would
rate Goodreads four out of five stars overall.
We then began the card sorting activities with her, again starting with categorizing an
assortment of features based on how frequently she uses them. Figure C.5 displays her
four-tiered ranking, which she ultimately also regarded as being equivalent to how she
would rank the features based on how much she enjoys using them. ”Participating in
reading challenges (or setting reading goals)” and ”discovering new books to read” were
positioned the highest. Two features that were not previously included until she added
them were: ”monthly trending books” and ”book choice awards”, which she place in the
third tier.
Figure C.6 exhibits Emma’s ranking of the personal statistics we presented to her, which
included ”how many different genres you’ve read (and which)”, ”most common qualitative
trends among the books you’ve read”, ”your progress towards your self-set reading goal”,
and ”average rating you’ve assigned to the books you’ve read” all in the highest tier. At the
bottom, she placed ”your average reading speed (and progress over time)”, saying that
reading is something she does for fun when she has time, not necessarily professionally.
Overall, even the highest ranked statistics were not very important according to Emma,
but she may occasionally peruse them if they’re available to her.
In the final card sorting activity about influential factors during book selection/discovery,
she ended up assigning only three different tiers rather than four, as seen in Figure C.7.

==PAGE:144==
Figure C.4: I1 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) influential factors of book

Among her top ranked included ”recommendations from friends, family, etc.” which she
explained further by saying, ”it’s nice because then you can talk about it... It might not be
[a new favorite book] but it’s like a shared experience.”
One of our final topics of discussion before closing the interview was about the Goodreads
search function, ”I guess you kind of have to know the name [of the book] beforehand,”
she thought aloud, ”It feels kind of old sometimes.”
I3 - William
We interviewed William third. At 23 years old, William rarely reads printed books. Instead
he uses his computer or reads e-books, usually about non-fiction topics like poker strategy
or mathematical books, but sometimes about science fiction or fantasy. He described
himself as someone who reads around eight total books per year, but often starts books
without finishing them. He finds it especially challenging to find books that really interest
him; which only becomes more challenging when he often doesn’t know what exactly he’s
looking for.
William does not nor has he ever used a dedicated book-tracking app. Instead, he keeps
a short list of want-to-read books in his phone’s notes app with titles usually recommended
to him by friends or discovered in internet forums. Because he only keeps a list of books
to read in the future and does not log the books he has previously read in any way, we
chose to regard William as a non-user. For this reason, we felt it was unnecessary to
present the first card sorting activity regarding common book-tracking features.
Alternatively, we moved right into the card sorting activity involving statistics. His resulting
138
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:145==
Figure C.5: I2 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) used features of book-tracking
categorization can be seen in Figure C.8, which includes ”path of creation of the book (why
was it made)” and ”most common qualities of the authors whose work you read” in the
top spot. The former was an additional statistic that he personally suggested including.
When asked about why he would want to know about the authors he reads from, William
explained that having an overview of their educational background and work experience
would help him manage his expectations better and provide a sense of credibility of the
authors.
While he had never heard of the term ”book-tracking” previously, William shared with us
that he is interested in finding an app that tracks and does data collection for him. He
mentioned that he would especially like if the tracking could then inform the recommen-
dations he receives, which could say something along the lines of ”there’s a 70% chance
you will enjoy this book.”
Our second and final card sorting activity with William involved the influential factors during
book selection/discovery. As is visible in Figure C.9, he created six tiers for this activity,
with the highest ranked factors being:
• ”recommendations from friends, family, etc.”,
• ”online data-based recommendations for you”,
• ”written online reviews”.
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
139
==PAGE:146==
Figure C.6: I2 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) interesting personal statistics
He subsequently added ”controversy attached,” a new factor, to his second tier as shown
in Figure C.10. Regarding controversial books, he told us, ”It’s completely new and com-
pletely opposite from standard literature... Is it good? Is it bad? I don’t know, but it also
caught my attention.”
We then asked William about how social he is when it comes to reading. In response, he
told us, ”it’s more of a personal experience.” He also said that he doesn’t often discuss
books with friends ”unless it’s motivational books, which are kind of popular right now,
then yeah, some of them we are discussing but not that much.”
In closing, William said that he’s generally not really interested in keeping a list of all his
past read books, but he would be interested in using a book-tracking platform for the
purposes of discovery and tracking.
I4 - Anna
Our fourth interview was with a 28 year old woman we’ll refer to as Anna. While she
mostly reads print books, Anna shared that she also occasionally reads audio-books or
borrows e-books for free using an app called Libby. Furthermore, she has recently started
to compound reading physical or e-books with the synchronized audio-book in order to
help her improve her focus and retention.
On average, Anna reads around one big book per month. She’s most interested in thriller
or suspense books, especially those with spies or war involved that she can then use to
learn something new. She also shared with us that reading in other languages has helped
her learn those languages. In terms of note-taking or annotating, Anna made it clear that
she refuses to mark up a print book, but will sometimes take photos or screenshots of
140
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:147==
Figure C.7: I2 card sorting results: most (A) to least (C) influential factors of book
discovery
pages and digitally highlight important parts to refer to later. One of her biggest frustrations
she shared with us is her feeling that she cannot retain more information from the books
she has read.
When describing her reading habits, Anna often referred to her dad and brother, reflecting
on how much they have guided and influenced her as a reader. ”This year I asked my
older brother (he reads a lot - way more than I read - and my dad is also on another level)...
how can I start again,” she shared with us, ”because last year it was really hard to keep
up with a book.” She expressed that she always trusts their recommendations.
Anna began book-tracking in 2017 when she started reading more often by manually
updating a page in her phone’s notes app. She typically writes the title, the year in which
she read the book, and the author. Additionally, she keeps a want-to-read list in another
page of her notes app. ”I think for me it started as a challenge... How many books can
you actually read?” Now, she also uses her notes app to sometimes keep track of context
changes (like shifting between multiple time periods) in the books she reads. Rating a
book is also unnecessary to her because she often remembers more or less what the
book was about and how she felt about each one, especially if she sees the book cover.
Keeping a log has even helped her overcome reading slumps, because she knows if
she’s struggling, she can revisit one of her known favorite books to re-energize herself
once again.

In regards to the most interesting statistics card sorting activity, Anna’s personal ranking
is shown in Figure C.11. She formed only two categories, indicating a ”yes” on the right
and a ”no” on the left column. Among her ”yes” statistics included the ”path of creation of
the book (why was it made),” which she explained adds character to the book.
We also had her do the card sorting activity involving influential factors for book selec-
tion/discovery, in which she ranked ”book cover or title” and ”recommendations from
friends, family, etc.” among others in her top priority group, as seen in Figure C.12. She
also included ”availability of book” as a new addition to the assortment of cards, explaining
that because she uses a library-based app called Libby to borrow certain books, many
are not available at a given time, which leaves her needing to explore different options.
As we concluded the interview, Anna shared with us that her current approach to book-
tracking is somehow working well enough for her, but there are certain aspects that she
would like to know more about.
I5 - Oliver
Our fifth and final interview of the week was with Oliver, a 23 year old self-proclaimed
”newcomer” to the world of reading. Oliver shared that reading used to feel like a chore
to him until a few months ago when he set a new year’s resolution to read one book per
month. This drive led him to Goodreads and its built-in reading challenge feature. He
described to us that he ”started seeing it almost as a game. It’s just about getting that
percentage as high as possible. And then I started doing that more and more, and I found
that I actually enjoyed most of the books that I had read.” Now, he finds reading to be less
of a chore and more of a leisure activity, and he’s way ahead of schedule. ”It’s a habit
now even though it’s only been two months or something.”
Oliver checks Goodreads around once per day. He has even gotten some of his friends
on board, although he says he doesn’t usually share similar taste in books with them. For
142
Improving UX in book-tracking platform through design thinking
==PAGE:149==
Figure C.9: I3 card sorting results: most (A) to least (F) influential factors of book
discovery
his needs, Oliver told us that Goodreads gets the job done and he would rate it five out of
five stars.
Because he uses a book-tracking app, we had Oliver do the card sorting activity that
involved categorizing common features based on his frequency of use. The resulting
three groups he created are displayed in Figure C.13. Although he told us he uses all of
them, Oliver’s most frequently used features included:
• ”rating/reviewing books you’ve read”,
• ”tracking different aspects of your reading habits”,
• ”reading reviews of other people”,
• ”discovering new books to read”,
• ”participating in reading challenges”,
• ”organizing the books you’ve read and want to read into collections”.
When asked how he would then rank these features based on enjoyment, he grouped
them as shown in Figure C.14, with ”participating in reading challenges” and ”tracking
different aspects of your reading habits” at the top. ”I love statistics...” he told us, ”It’s
interesting to see what kinds of patterns I follow.”
This led us into the next card sorting activity, which was to categorize a number of statistics
based on interest level. Figure C.15 showcases Oliver’s ranking. In the top category, he
included:
• ”average rating you’ve assigned to the books you’ve read”,
• ”total number of books/pages read each year”,
• ”most common qualitative trends among the books you’ve read”,
• ”how many different genres you’ve read (and which)”,
• total time you’ve spent reading”,

• ”your average reading speed (and progress over time)”,
• ”your progress towards your self-set reading goal”.
Of the statistics in the bottom category, he said that it wasn’t that he did not care about

Our final card sorting activity with Oliver involved the influential factors for book selec-
tion/discovery, to which his thoughts are reflected in Figure C.16. He added a new card
to the assortment, which read, ”difficulty of reading (vocab, etc.)”, explaining that if the
writing style of a book is too advanced, he’s often discouraged from reading it and will
instead look for other options. He ranked this factor highly, along with the following:
• ”online data-based recommendations for you”,
• ”summaries or book descriptions”,
• ”the number of and average ratings”,
• ”author”,
• ”displays of similar books to what you’ve previously read”,
• ”genre”,
• ”written online reviews”,
• ”recommendations from friends, family, etc.”.
Overall, Oliver shared with us that he has been having a generally positive experience
using Goodreads and is grateful that he no longer views reading as a chore.

needs/wants related to reviews
SEARCH: 
PERSONA A usually reads reviews after reading the book so as not to be spoiled or biased. 
PERSONA B suspects that some reviews may be bought.
PERSONA C wishes there were different ways of filtering reviews.
PERSONA D cares more to read reviews of friends or people with similar taste rather than strangers. 
PERSONA E is interested in seeing more about the path of creation of the book and the author. 
PERSONA F thinks that book summaries can be misleading and not show the full picture.
CATALOG: 
PERSONA A manually tracks TBR list in notes app of phone.
PERSONA B uses a BT app but finds the organizing/collections 
feature to be messy and not intuitive. They dislike not being able 
to provide context for books on TBR list (why is this book here).
PERSONA C enjoys using BT app to organize books but wishes 
he could add books manually if not in app’s database.
wishes you could provide 
context to TBR list books 
(prioritize?)
DECIDE: 
PERSONA A sometimes doesn’t know really 
what he’s looking for in a book until he’s 
reading it. 
PERSONA B forgets info about their TBR 
books and has to re-familiarize with each 
book before choosing.
PERSONA C doesn’t remember why she 
added certain books to her TBR (Was it 
recommended by a friend? Did she see it on 
IG?)
READ: 
PERSONA A often doesn’t finish books.
PERSONA B likes to highlight quotes and 
bookmark pages to come back to later. 
PERSONA C likes to do external research 
about topics they read about in books.
PERSONA D reads a combination of physical, 
digital, and audiobooks often.
PERSONA E usually reads on the go (train, 
bike, etc.).
JUDGE BOOK: 
PERSONA A really values good writing style 
and books that make her think. 
PERSONA B likes to remember how he felt 
about past books. 
PERSONA C feels that rating and reviewing 
books would only be worth his time if it 
helped the system recommend better books.
RECOMMEND:
PERSONA A recommends books to her 
friends in person or through messages rather 
than the app.
DISCUSS:
PERSONA A feels that reading is a personal 
experience so rarely discusses books. If 
anything - motivational books. 
PERSONA B likes to discuss books with 
friends.
PERSONA C likes informal book clubs to read 
and talk about agreed books.
REFLECTING ON READING HABITS:
PERSONA A feels that tracking is too 
competitive and stressful.
PERSONA B really wants to read more.
PERSONA C wants to read different types of 
books from time to time.
PERSONA D wants to track his reading 
speed/time spent reading but he primarily 
reads print books. 
PERSONA E really enjoys gamification.
PERSONA F really enjoys seeing insights 
about themselves in a simple, visual way.
PERSONA G often forgets to manually input 
data but wants to track their reading habits.
DISCOVER:
PERSONA A wishes there was a better way 
to search for new books to read. 
PERSONA B finds that the online system 
recommendations suck. 
PERSONA C really likes to see what friends 
are reading but doesn’t want to spam them.
PERSONA D likes to browse bestseller lists 
but doesn’t feel they’re super reliable.
PERSONA E feels lost/overwhelmed in 
physical bookstores but prefers reading 
physical books.
PERSONA F really loves recommendations 
from friends but rarely gets them.

const store = require('app-store-scraper');
2
const fs = require('fs');
3
4
const ids = {
5
'gr': 355833469,
6
}
7
const currentSort = store.sort.HELPFUL;
8
const currentId = ids.gr;
9
10
// Get 10 pages of reviews, combine them into one array, and write to file
11
let reviews = [];
12
for (let i = 1; i <= 10; i++) {
13
store
14
.reviews({
15
id: currentId,
16
sort: currentSort,
17
page: i,
18
})
19
.then((responseString) => {
20
reviews = reviews.concat(responseString);
21
fs.writeFileSync(`reviews.${currentSort}.json`, JSON.stringify(
reviews));
22
})
23
.catch(console.log);
24
}

ITERATION 1 TESTING  
Three users: 
- 
P1 – Print-only archetype 
- 
P2 – Print/Novice archetype 
- 
P3 – Print/Novice archetype 
 
 
⁃ 
Default shelves  
⁃ 
P1 - collections in collections might be 
confusing  
⁃ 
P1 -it could be collections just divided by 
genre and no separate “want to read” or 
“read” shelves, and then the titles that you 
have read look different from the titles you 
haven’t read  
⁃ 
P1 -Maybe the collections themselves 
can have tags and then when you search, you 
can search by tags that are associated with the 
books  
⁃ 
P2 - I don’t like too many filters and 
categories. I would be fine with general 
categories within each of the default shelves 
but I just don’t want to be presented with a 
bunch of categories at once. It makes me feel 
lost.  
⁃ 
GAB - So basically, you want to be able 
to easily find certain types of books within your 
shelves 
⁃ 
Grid vs list vs shelf view (VISUAL) 
⁃ 
P1 - the grid and list are nice, especially 
because seeing the cover would be most 
important to me. It’s also nice to have the 
possibility to scroll faster or have more details 
if you want to.  
⁃ 
P2 - what about like a tag line for each 
book? like a pitch?  
⁃ 
GAB - (told them about our user 
research and how many forget why they’ve 
added certain books to their list) So there’s the 
idea of being able to add some quick note for 
context when adding the book 
⁃ 
ALL - agreed and liked the idea 
⁃ 
P1 - It could be that you have to click on
